Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Mukatab (Book of the Mukatab) in Ashraf ul-Hadaya explains the rulings concerning the contract of kitabah (deed of emancipation) when the master dies. The passage is structured as a commentary on the Hidayah, and it covers three main points: (1) the effect of the master's death on the kitabah contract, (2) the payment obligation of the mukatab to the heirs, and (3) the possibility of manumission by the heirs. The author begins by stating that the kitabah contract remains valid after the master's death; it is not rescinded. This is because kitabah is a cause of freedom, and freedom is the right of the mukatab. Once a right is established, it is not extinguished by death, analogous to a debt that survives the creditor's death. The heirs step into the master's place solely for the purpose of collecting the installments of the kitabah payment. This is not a change in the contract but merely a substitution in collection. The text then addresses the scenario where one heir tries to manumit the mukatab individually. According to the Hanafi school, this is ineffective because the mukatab is not owned by any of the heirs individually; the mukatab is not subject to ownership through any cause, including inheritance. The text contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, quoting from Sharh al-Aqta' that Imam al-Shafi'i in one opinion allows a single heir to manumit the mukatab because, in his school, the mukatab can be sold and owned by the master. The Hanafi position relies on the principle that the mukatab is not owned. However, if all the heirs collectively manumit the mukatab, the ruling by qiyas (analogy) would be that it is still invalid because they do not own him. But by istihsan (juristic preference), it is valid and the mukatab becomes free, and the kitabah payment is dropped. The reason for istihsan is that the collective manumission is effectively a discharge from the debt of the kitabah payment, which is their right. Since they have inherited the right to the payment, they can forgo it. Once the debt is discharged, the mukatab is free, just as if the master had released him from the debt. The text concludes by addressing a potential objection: why does a single heir's manumission not have a similar effect, since it could be seen as a discharge from his share? The answer is that the discharge implied by manumission is for the entire debt, not a partial share. Manumission cannot be established partially; it either exists wholly or not at all. Therefore, only the collective act of all heirs can effect manumission through the mechanism of discharge. This passage is a classic example of Hanafi use of qiyas and istihsan in the context of contracts of emancipation.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum.
بدل کتابت (badal al-kitabah): The payment (installments) owed by the mukatab to the master under the kitabah contract.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.
ابراء (ibra'): Discharge from a debt; forgiveness of a financial obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if all heirs collectively manumit the mukatab after the master's death?
A) The manumission is invalid by qiyas, but valid by istihsan, and the kitabah payment is dropped.
B) The manumission is invalid because the heirs do not own the mukatab.
C) The manumission is valid only if the mukatab agrees to pay the remaining instalments.
D) The manumission is valid by qiyas because the heirs inherit the master's right.

Key Takeaway
A single heir cannot free the mukatab; only collective manumission by all heirs is valid, treated as a discharge of the kitabah debt.

The Legal Definition of Wala': Kinship or Mutual Support?
Defines wala' linguistically and legally with scholarly critiques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definition of wala' in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between the two scholarly interpretations (tanasur vs qarabah).

Core Idea
Wala' in Hanafi law is kinship-like (qarabah), not merely mutual support, as derived from linguistic proximity and Prophetic hadith.

Wala' is a key concept in Hanafi inheritance law, establishing a legal connection between a freed slave and his patron. But what exactly is the nature of that connection? In this passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah, scholars debate whether wala' means mutual support (tanasur) or kinship (qarabah). The resolution of this dispute has practical consequences for how wala' rights are applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Linguistic Derivation
Examine the passage's statement that wala' is derived from waliya meaning proximity. This is the foundation for the definition.
Linguistic analysis is a tool used by Hanafi fuqaha to determine legal meanings.
2
Understand the Two Types of Wala'
Note that wala' is divided into wala' al-'itaqah (from emancipation) and wala' al-muwalat (from a contract of alliance). The passage explains why both are called wala'.
3
Examine the Tanasur Definition and Its Critique
Some scholars, including the author of al-Mabsut, define wala' as mutual support (tanasur). The passage presents a critique from Sahib al-Nata'ij: what is sought (tanasur) is not the same as the thing itself.
This is a logical objection: the purpose is not the definition.
4
Analyze the Sharh al-'Inayah View and Its Flaw
Sharh al-'Inayah claims wala' means help/love and derives from proximity, but the author points out a contradiction: proximity yields kinship, not help.
Derivation must be consistent in both word and meaning.
5
Conclude with the Preferred Hanafi View
The passage endorses the view in al-Kafi wa al-Kifayah that wala' means kinship (qarabah), supported by the hadith: 'al-wala' luhmatun kaluhmatin nasab'.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya Volume 12. Book of Wala. Statement: Kitab al-Salkh - After Kitab al-Mukatab, the Book of Wala comes. Because wala is a substitute for the payment of kitabah at the time of payment through the removal of ownership of the slave's person; it is among the effects of kitabah. Although it is also among the effects of emancipation, the arrangement of previous books and chapters necessitated mentioning kitabah until here, making it necessary to delay the Book of Wala after the Book of al-Mukatab so that the effect is not preceded by the cause. Statement: al-wala... Wala linguistically is derived from waliya yali and du'iya (and rare from bab darb), meaning closeness and proximity: the second thing comes after the first without interval. It is said: 'Daruhu waliya dari' (his house is near my house). And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Let those of you who are wise and forbearing be near me.' And 'itaqah and wala al-muwalat are called wala because the ruling of both, i.e., inheritance, occurs at the time of the existence of the condition without interval. Some scholars have mentioned that wala and wala mean help and love, but in Shari'ah it is specifically for wala al-'itaq and wala al-muwalat. So wala in Shari'ah is mutual support, whether through emancipation or through contract of muwalat. For this reason, in al-Mabsut it is said: 'What is sought by each of them is mutual support.' Sahib al-Nata'ij says there is deficiency and infirmity in this speech, because the mentioned expression of al-Mabsut indicates that mutual support is other than wala al-'itaqah and wala al-muwalat, not that mutual support is exactly wala. This is because it is quite obvious that what is sought from a thing is not exactly that thing, but is other than it, since there is no meaning for something being a means to itself. So the expression of al-Mabsut does not fully support the statement that in Shari'ah wala al-'itaqah and wala al-muwalat are mutual support. In Sharh al-'Inayah it is said that wala in language is help and love, and it is derived from wali meaning proximity, and among Arab jurists it is that which necessitates inheritance and reason, i.e., mutual support. But this statement is also not free from flaw. Because wala which is derived from wali meaning proximity cannot be help and love; rather it would be kinship. Because derivation requires proportion between the two words in both word and meaning. However, there is no semantic proportion between wali meaning proximity and wala meaning help and love; rather there is verbal and semantic proportion between wali meaning proximity and wala meaning kinship. For this reason, in al-Kafi wa al-Kifayah it is said: 'It is from al-wali meaning proximity, and it is said: between them is wala, i.e., kinship, and from it is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): Wala is like the bond of lineage, i.e., connection like the connection of lineage.'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a definition and linguistic analysis of wala' from the commentary on Hidayah. The author begins by explaining why the Book of Wala comes after the Book of al-Mukatab: because wala arises from the payment of kitabah (contract of manumission), and it is an effect of that contract. Then he gives the linguistic derivation: wala' is from waliya, meaning closeness. The Prophet's hadith 'Let those of you who are wise and forbearing be near me' illustrates linguistic usage. Both wala' al-'itaqah (emancipation) and wala' al-muwalat (alliance) are called wala' because the right of inheritance occurs immediately upon the condition being fulfilled. Next, a critique is presented: some scholars define wala' as mutual support (tanasur). The author of al-Mabsut said the sought objective of both types is mutual support. Sahib al-Nata'ij objects that the sought objective is not the same as the thing itself; mutual support is the goal, not the definition. Similarly, Sharh al-'Inayah defines wala' as help and love, but the author points out a flaw: if wala' is derived from proximity, it should mean kinship, not help. The correct view, as in al-Kafi wa al-Kifayah, is that wala' means kinship, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Wala' is like the bond of lineage.' Thus, wala' is a legal connection that brings rights of inheritance and mutual responsibility.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Linguistically closeness; legally, the right of inheritance and mutual responsibility arising from emancipation or contract of alliance.
تناصر (tanasur): Mutual help and support; some scholars define wala' as tanasur.
ولاء عتاقة (wala' al-'itaqah): Patronage resulting from emancipation of a slave who becomes free.
ولاء موالاۃ (wala' al-muwalat): Patronage resulting from a contract of alliance between two free persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what does wala' mean linguistically and legally?
A) Mutual help and support
B) Closeness and kinship
C) A contract of alliance
D) Emancipation

Key Takeaway
Wala' in Hanafi law is a kinship-like legal bond, not merely mutual support, established through emancipation or alliance, and it entitles the patron to inherit from the freed slave.

Proofs and Two Types of Wala'
Establishes wala' from Quran and Sunnah and divides it into two types with causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two types of wala' and their causes, and explain why the correct Hanafi view holds that ownership, not emancipation, is the cause of wala' al-'itaqah.

Core Idea
Wala' is like lineage; its cause is ownership at freedom, not the act of emancipation.

This lesson establishes the legal principle of wala' (clientage) from the Quran and hadith, then clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute: what is the cause (sabab) of wala' al-'itaqah? You will learn to distinguish the two types of wala' and apply the correct ruling, understanding that ownership at the time of freedom, not the act of emancipation, determines the right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept and its sources
Recognize that wala' is a relationship of inheritance and mutual support established by Quran and Sunnah. The Quranic verse (33:5) and four hadith are cited, including 'al-wala' liman a'taqa'.
The Quran uses 'mawaleekum' (your clients) to indicate wala'.
2
Differentiate the two types of wala'
Classify wala' into Wala al-Itaqah (patronage from emancipation) and Wala al-Muwalat (contractual alliance). Their causes are ownership at freedom and contract, respectively.
Wala al-Muwalat is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the cause of wala' al-'itaqah
Some Hanafi associates argue the cause is the act of emancipation (i'taq) based on the hadith. The correct view is that it is ownership at the time of freedom. Evidence: if a relative inherits a slave who becomes free by operation of law, the wala' goes to the heir, even without any emancipation act.
The text states 'al-sahih' (the correct view) is ownership.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a scenario where an heir inherits a slave who becomes free by operation of law (e.g., a relative), determine who gets the wala'. The correct view: the heir gets it because he owned the slave at the moment of freedom.
This demonstrates that ownership, not emancipation, is the cause.

Benefit: The establishment of the origin of wala is found in both Quran and Hadith. Allah Most High says: 'But if you know not their fathers, then they are your brothers in religion and your clients (mawaleekum).' Hadith are also abundant in this regard: (1) The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Wala belongs to the one who emancipates.' (Sahihain) (2) The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of wala and its gift. (Also from Ibn Umar) (3) The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'May Allah curse the one who takes as client other than his own mawali.' (Tirmidhi) (4) From Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to me: 'Wala is like the bond of lineage; it cannot be sold nor given as a gift.' (Ibn Jarir in Tahdhib al-Athar) He said: Wala is of two types: Wala al-Itaqah, also called Wala al-Ni'mah, and its cause, according to the correct view, is emancipation while owned. So even if a relative becomes free by inheritance, the wala is for him. And Wala al-Muwalah, its cause is contract. Therefore it is called Wala al-Itaqah and Wala al-Muwalah, and the ruling is attributed to its cause. The meaning in both is mutual support. The Arabs used to support each other through various things, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) confirmed their mutual support through both types of wala. He said: 'Indeed, the mawla of a people is from them, and their ally is from them.' By 'ally' is meant mawla al-muwalah, because they used to emphasize muwalah with an oath. Translation: Wala is of two types: Wala al-Itaqah, also called Wala al-Ni'mah. Its cause, according to the correct view, is emancipation on his ownership. So even if his close relative becomes free due to being an heir, his wala will also go to him. And Wala al-Muwalah, its cause is contract. Therefore it is called Wala al-Itaqah and Wala al-Muwalah, and the ruling is attributed to its cause. The meaning in both is mutual support. The Arabs used to support each other through many things, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) confirmed their mutual support through both types of wala. So he said: 'The mawla of a people is from them, and the ally of a people is from them.' By 'mawla' is meant mawla al-muwalah, because the Arabs used to emphasize muwalah with an oath. Explanation: Statement: Wala is of two types etc. In Shari'ah terminology, wala is the entitlement to inheritance that is obtained from a freed slave or through a contract of muwalah. It has two types: Wala al-Itaqah and Wala al-Muwalah. Wala al-Itaqah is also called Wala al-Ni'mah, following the verse about Zaid ibn Harith: 'And when you said to the one upon whom Allah has bestowed favor and upon whom you have bestowed favor.' That is: Allah bestowed upon him Islam, and you bestowed upon him emancipation. Statement: And its cause is emancipation etc. The cause of Wala al-Itaqah, according to the correct view, is that the slave becomes free while in his ownership, whether by his own emancipation or otherwise. By the phrase 'in the correct view', he avoids the saying of some of our associates that its cause is emancipation (i'taq) based on the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa.' The reason for avoidance is that the aforementioned deduction is weak. Because if a person owns his close relative, he becomes free (by operation of law), even if by inheritance, and his wala goes to him, even though he did not himself emancipate; rather he became free by entitlement. So the correct view is that its cause is ownership (mulk), not emancipation. And for the second type, Wala al-Muwalah, its cause is contract, i.e., making a covenant of muwalah. Therefore, wala is attributed to its cause and called Wala al-Itaqah and Wala al-Muwalah, and not Wala al-I'tiqaq.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah commentary. It begins with a benefit (fa'idah) establishing the origin of wala from the Quranic verse: 'If you know not their fathers, then they are your brothers in religion and your clients' (Quran 33:5). Then four hadith are cited: (1) 'al-Wala' liman a'taqa' (Wala belongs to the one who emancipates) from Sahihain; (2) prohibition of selling or gifting wala; (3) curse on those who take clients from other than their own; (4) the hadith that wala is like the bond of lineage, cannot be sold or given. Then the text divides wala into two types: (1) Wala al-Itaqah (also called Wala al-Ni'mah) — its cause is emancipation while the slave is owned. A crucial intra-Hanafi clarification follows: some associates (as-hab) said the cause is the act of emancipation (i'taq) based on the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa.' However, the correct view (al-sahih) is that the cause is ownership of the slave (mulk) at the time of freedom. Evidence: if a relative inherits a slave who then becomes free by operation of law (e.g., a relative becoming free due to ownership), the wala goes to the heir, even though the heir did not perform the emancipation. Therefore, the cause is ownership, not the act of emancipation. (2) Wala al-Muwalah — its cause is a contract of alliance (muwalah). The term 'wala' is attributed to its cause, so it is called 'Wala al-Itaqah' and 'Wala al-Muwalah,' not 'Wala al-I'tiqaq.' The underlying meaning in both types is mutual support (tanasur). The Arabs used various means for mutual support, and the Prophet confirmed their practice through both types of wala, as he said: 'The mawla of a people is from them, and their ally is from them.' The 'ally' (halif) refers to mawla al-muwalah, because Arabs used to strengthen muwalah with an oath.

Key Terms
ولاء عتاقة (wala' al-'itaqah): The right of inheritance and patronage that arises when a slave owned by a person becomes free, whether by that person's emancipation or by operation of law.
ولاء موالاۃ (wala' al-muwalat): A contractual right of inheritance and mutual support between two free persons who are not related by lineage or emancipation.
الولاء لمن اعتق (al-wala' liman a'taqa): A hadith meaning wala belongs to the one who emancipates; used by some as proof that the cause is the act of emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, what is the cause (sabab) of wala' al-'itaqah?
A) The act of emancipation (i'taq)
B) A contract of alliance
C) Ownership (mulk) of the slave at the time of freedom
D) Inheritance by a relative

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi view: wala' al-'itaqah stems from ownership at the time of freedom, not the act of emancipation, as shown by inherited slavery cases.

Definition and Two Types of Wala' in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and division of wala' into two types with hadith evidence and intra-Hanafi clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wala' in Hanafi fiqh, distinguish its two types, state the correct cause of wala' al-itaqah, and explain why wala' cannot be sold or gifted.

Core Idea
Wala' is a non-transferable bond of support that creates inheritance rights; it arises from manumission or contract.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi concept of wala' — a unique legal relationship that grants inheritance and support rights. The text defines two types: wala' al-itaqah (from manumission) and wala' al-muwalat (from contract). It also clarifies a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement about the precise cause of wala' al-itaqah. Armed with this, you will understand both the rules and the reasoning behind this pre-Islamic custom that Islam reformed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of wala' and their causes
From the text, list wala' al-itaqah (cause: becoming free while owned) and wala' al-muwalat (cause: contract).
Also note the alternate name: wala' al-ni'mah for wala' al-itaqah.
2
Understand the correct Hanafi view on the cause of wala' al-itaqah
The correct view is that the cause is the reality of freedom from ownership, not the act of manumission. The example of a relative becoming free by inheritance demonstrates this.
This differs from some Hanafi companions who hold the cause is the act of manumission based on the hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taq'.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence prohibiting sale/gift of wala'
The text quotes several hadith forbidding the sale and gift of wala', indicating it is an inalienable right.
The prohibition is explicit, making wala' non-transferable.
4
Grasp the purpose (tanasur) that the Prophet confirmed
The purpose of both types is mutual support (tanasur), which the Prophet confirmed as a legitimate basis for inclusion in the tribe.
This support specifically includes inheritance and blood wit liability.

قوله ان مولى القوم منهم ... الخ - يحدیث چار صحابہ کرام رفاعہ بن رافع زرقی، ابو ہریرہ ، عمرو بن عوف ، اور عقبہ بن غزوان سے مروی ہے حدیث رفاعہ کی تخریج امام احمد نے مسند میں ۔ ابن ابی شیبہ نے مصنف میں طبرانی تیم میں اور حاکم نے مستدرک میں کی ہے اور صحیح الاسناد کہا ہے۔ قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مولى القوم منهم وابن اختهم منهم وحليفهم منهم
امام بخاری نے المفروقی الادب میں ایک قصہ بھی بیان کیا ہے۔ حدیث ابوہریرہ کی تخریج بزار نے مسند میں کی ہے:
عن النبي قال: حليف القوم منهم وابن اختهم منهم
حدیث عمرو بن عوف کی تخریج دارمی، ابن ابی شیبہ، ابن راہویہ نے اپنے مسانید میں ۔ طبرانی نے معجم میں اور ابراہیم حربی نے کتاب

قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ابن اخت القوم منهم وحليف القوم منهم
The Book of Wala'. (2) The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the sale and gift of wala' (also from Ibn Umar). (3) The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: May Allah curse one who takes as mawla someone other than his own mawla (Tirmidhi). From Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa: The Prophet said to me: Wala' is like a tie of lineage; it cannot be sold or given. Two types of wala'. Ibn Jarir in Tahdhib al-Athar: He said: Wala' is of two types: wala' al-itaqah, also called wala' al-ni'mah, and its cause is manumission while owned, according to the correct view; even if his relative becomes free by inheritance, wala' belongs to him. And wala' al-muwalat, its cause is contract; therefore it is called wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat. The ruling is added to its cause. The meaning in both is mutual support. The Arabs used to support each other through various things, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) established their mutual support through both types of wala', so he said: The mawla of a people is from them, and their ally is from them. What is meant by 'ally' is the mawla of muwalat, because they used to reinforce muwalat with oaths. Translation in Urdu: Wala' is of two types: wala' al-itaqah, also called wala' al-ni'mah; its cause according to the correct view is freedom from his ownership, so that if his close relative becomes free due to inheritance, his wala' is also obtained. And wala' al-muwalat, its cause is contract; therefore it is called wala' al-itaqah and al-muwalat. The ruling is added to its cause, and the meaning in both is mutual support. The Arabs used to mutually support each other through many things, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) established their mutual support through both types of wala'. He said: The mawla of a people is from them, and the ally of a people is from them. What is meant by mawla is mawla al-muwalat, because the Arabs used to reinforce muwalat with oaths. Commentary: His saying 'wala' is of two types' etc. In legal terminology, wala' refers to the entitlement to inheritance that is obtained from a freed slave or through a contract of muwalat. It has two types: wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat. Wala' al-itaqah is also called wala' al-ni'mah, following the verse about Zayd ibn Harith: 'And when you said to the one upon whom Allah bestowed favor and you bestowed favor' meaning Allah bestowed favor upon him with Islam and you bestowed favor upon him with manumission. His saying 'and its cause is manumission' etc. The cause of wala' al-itaqah, according to the correct view, is reality, i.e., that the slave is freed from his ownership, whether by his manumission or otherwise. By 'the correct view' it avoids the statement of some of our companions that its cause is the act of manumission, based on the hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taq'. The reason for avoidance is that the mentioned evidence is weak. Because if a person owns a close relative, he becomes free upon him, even through inheritance, and his wala' is also obtained by him, even though he himself did not manumit; rather, the slave became free by entitlement. Thus, the correct view is that its cause is the reality, not the act of manumission. And the second type, wala' al-muwalat, its cause is contract, i.e., making a covenant of mutual loyalty. Therefore, wala' is attributed to its cause and called wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat, not wala' al-i'taq. (page break) The Book of Wala'. Ashraf al-Bidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah, vol. 12. His saying 'and the meaning in both' etc. Sahib al-Inaya's opinion is that 'and the meaning in both is mutual support' intends to explain the legal meaning of wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat. But according to the author of Nata'ij, this is debatable because their legal meaning is not absolute mutual support, but rather the mutual support that entails inheritance and blood wit. So if the author of Hidayah intended to explain the legal meaning, he would not have kept mutual support absolute, but would have specified it with what is considered in their meaning. Also, if he intended to explain their meaning absolutely, whether linguistic or legal, he would have said 'and their meaning is mutual support'. Thus, the correct view is that the author of Hidayah intended to express that which is sought from wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat. Therefore, the author of al-Kafi said instead of 'and the meaning in both is mutual support': 'and the sought in each of them is mutual support', and it occurs similarly in al-Mabsut. In any case, the meaning of the statement is that what is intended and sought from wala' al-itaqah and wala' al-muwalat is mutual support, i.e., helping and supporting one another. Before the Prophet (peace be upon him), the Arabs used to support each other through many things: kinship, friendship, alliance, oath, asabah, wala' al-itaqah, wala' al-muwalat. The Prophet (peace be upon him) established mutual support through both types of wala'. He said: 'The mawla of a people is from them.' His saying 'indeed, the mawla of a people is from them' etc. This hadith is narrated from four companions: Rifa'ah ibn Rafi' al-Zuraqi, Abu Hurairah, Amr ibn Awf, and Uqbah ibn Ghazwan. The hadith of Rifa'ah is recorded by Ahmad in his Musnad, Ibn Abi Shaybah in his Musannaf, al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam, and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak, who declared it sahih al-isnad. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The mawla of a people is from them, and their sister's son is from them, and their ally is from them.' Imam Bukhari narrated a story in al-Adab al-Mufrad. The hadith of Abu Hurairah is recorded by al-Bazzar in his Musnad: from the Prophet: 'The ally of a people is from them, and their sister's son is from them.' The hadith of Amr ibn Awf is recorded by al-Darimi, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and Ibn Rahawayh in their Musnads, al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam, and Ibrahim al-Harbi in Gharib al-Hadith; after the story it says: So the Messenger of Allah said: 'The sister's son of a people is from them, and their ally is from them, and their mawla is from them.' The hadith of Uqbah is recorded by al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam: The Messenger of Allah said: 'The sister's son of a people is from them, and the ally of a people is from them.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines wala' and its two types in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that wala' cannot be sold or given as a gift, citing a hadith. Then it explains that wala' is of two types: wala' al-itaqah (by manumission) and wala' al-muwalat (by contract). The cause of wala' al-itaqah is the fact that a slave becomes free while owned by another, even if the freedom occurs by inheritance rather than direct manumission. The correct view (as opposed to some Hanafi scholars who hold the cause is the act of manumission) is that the cause is the realization of freedom from ownership. This is supported by the example of a person who owns a relative and the relative becomes free by inheritance; the wala' belongs to the owner even though he did not manumit. The cause of wala' al-muwalat is a contract between two free persons for mutual support. The passage then explains that the purpose of both types is mutual support (tanasur), a pre-Islamic practice that the Prophet confirmed and regulated. Several hadith are cited to show that a mawla (through muwalat) and an ally are considered part of the tribe. The commentary further clarifies that the legal meaning of tanasur here is specifically the support that results in inheritance rights and blood wit liability, not just any mutual assistance. The author then provides detailed chains of transmission for the hadith, showing their authenticity.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): The entitlement to inheritance and support from a freed slave or a contractual ally.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-itaqah): Wala' resulting from manumission of a slave, also called wala' al-ni'mah (loyalty of favor).
ولاء الموالاة (wala' al-muwalat): Wala' resulting from a contract of mutual loyalty and support between two free individuals.
تناصر (tanasur): Mutual support and assistance, which is the purpose of both types of wala'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, which of the following is the cause of wala' al-itaqah?
A) The act of manumission (i'taq)
B) The contract of muwalat
C) The slave becoming free while owned (al-'itq 'ala milk)
D) The agreement of the tribe

Key Takeaway
Wala' is a bond of mutual support that creates inheritance and blood wit rights; it arises from either manumission (wala' al-itaqah) or contract (wala' al-muwalat) and cannot be sold or gifted.

intermediate

Wala' Belongs to the Emancipator: Ruling, Proof, and Effects
Ruling with proof: establishes the bond of wala' and its effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that wala' is exclusive to the emancipator, explain the proof from the hadith of Barira using linguistic analysis, and identify the two legal effects (inheritance and aqila) along with their justifications.

Core Idea
The bond of wala' is exclusive to the emancipator and cannot be transferred or sold.

This lesson unpacks a foundational ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when a master frees a slave, the bond of clientage (wala') belongs solely to the emancipator. The Prophet's clear statement in the hadith of Barira establishes this as a text-based rule, reinforced by a broad consensus. We will examine the precise linguistic evidence, the two key effects of wala'—inheritance and blood-money liability—and address a minority view that analogizes wala' to inheritable property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Legal Level
State the core ruling: wala' belongs exclusively to the emancipator. Note that this is a wajib ruling established by sunnah, not a conditional right that can be bargained away.
The ruling is absolute; any stipulation to give wala' to another is void.
2
Locate and Analyze the Primary Dalil
The hadith of Barira: 'al-wala' liman a'taqa'. Break down the Arabic: 'al-wala'' with lam of genus (al-jins) and 'liman a'taqa' with lam of specification (al-ikhtisas). Explain how this construction indicates exclusivity, analogous to 'al-hamdu lillah'.
The thubut is zanni (ahad) but the dalala is qati' (clear meaning).
3
Identify the Two Effects of Wala'
Effect 1: Inheritance (irth) – the emancipator inherits from the freed slave if no Qur'anic heirs exist. Effect 2: Aqila (blood money) – the emancipator and his clan pay diya for the freed slave's accidental homicide. Note the justifications: mutual support (al-tanassur) for aqila, and the maxim al-ghurm bi al-ghurm (liability in exchange for liability) for inheritance.
The emancipator's responsibility mirrors the benefit he receives.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf and Hanafi Response
Some early jurists (e.g., Shuraih) analogized wala' to inheritable property, allowing transfer. The Hanafi response: the hadith text is clear and specific; analogy (qiyas) cannot override a textual ruling. The majority of Companions, Tabi'un, and later Imams follow the exclusivity view.
This ikhtilaf has a practical impact on conditions in emancipation contracts.

Book of Wala'. Translation: When a master frees his slave, his wala' belongs to the master. Due to the prophetic command: 'Wala' belongs to the one who frees.' And because emancipation leads to mutual support, so he (the master) will also pay his (the freed slave's) blood money. And the master has revived him metaphorically by removing his slavery, so he will also inherit him. And wala' becomes like lineage. And because benefit is in exchange for liability. Similarly, a woman who frees [gets wala'], by the evidence of the hadith we narrated. And the freed slave of Hamza's daughter left behind his female patron and a daughter, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided his property between them half and half. And it is equal whether the freeing is for compensation or without compensation due to the absolute nature of the hadith we mentioned. Explanation of his statement 'and when he frees...': When a master frees his slave and he dies leaving no heir, then his estate goes to the emancipator, because the six authentic compilers narrated from Aisha: 'When she bought Barira, her family stipulated that her wala' be for them. Aisha asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he said: Free her, for wala' belongs to the one who frees.' When Aisha bought Barira, her people stipulated that her wala' would be for them. Aisha asked the Prophet (peace be upon him). He said: Free her. Indeed, wala' is for the one who frees. The reasoning: 'al-wala'' has the lam of genus and 'liman a'taqa' has the lam of specification; both indicate that the genus of wala' is for the emancipator and not for others, like the verse 'al-hamdu lillah' where scholars said the lam of genus and lam of specification indicate that all praises are specific to Allah. In any case, wala' is only for the emancipator and does not transfer. This is narrated from Umar, Ali, Zayd ibn Thabit, Ibn Mas'ud, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Ibn Umar, Abu Mas'ud al-Badri, and Usama ibn Zayd. Also Ata', Tawus, Salim ibn Abdullah, Hasan al-Basri, Ibn Sirin, al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, al-Zuhri, Qatada, Abu al-Zinad, Ibn Nushayt, Sufyan al-Thawri, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, Imam Malik, Imam al-Shafi'i, and the Hanafis all hold this view, and from Ahmad a group also narrated it. And Shuraih said: 'Wala' is like wealth inherited from the emancipator; whoever owns something during his life, it is for his heirs after his death.' The statement 'and because mutual support': the establishment of wala' has two effects: one is inheritance and the other is aqila (blood money). The proof for the second is 'because mutual support in his aqila'. And the proof for the first is 'because liability is in exchange for liability'. The explanation: through emancipation, mutual support is obtained. So if the master sought help from him, he also bears the penalty for his error. Thus, if the freed slave kills someone by mistake, the master along with his clan bears the blood money. Then, before that, the master had removed his slavery and metaphorically revived him, because slavery is an effect of disbelief, and disbelief is a figurative death. Do you not see the verse: 'Or he who was dead and We gave him life'? And the slave is legally dead; so many rulings related to life are not established for him, such as being a judge, bearing witness, attending Friday prayer, going out for the two Eids, and owning property. Through emancipation, all these rulings become established for him. So this is a metaphorical revival. And whoever gives someone metaphorical life becomes his heir. Thus wala' became like a lineage relationship, just as a child's life is caused by the father, so he inherits him. Similarly, the emancipator will inherit the freed slave. Also, benefit is in exchange for liability, and the master bears liability due to him, so he is entitled to his benefit. Question: If the master leaves no 'asaba relative, then the freed slave should inherit from the master, as Hasan ibn Ziyad, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and Bishr al-Marisi hold, but the general scholars disagree. Answer: The freed slave is a complete stranger to the master, and the text came contrary to qiyas regarding the freed slave, so one cannot analogize others to him.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya explains the foundational ruling of the Book of Wala': when a master frees a slave, the wala' (patronage/clientage) belongs exclusively to the emancipator. The passage type is a ruling with proof, expanding on the matn of al-Hidayah. The author first presents the ruling in Urdu translation, then provides the primary proof from the hadith of Barira (narrated by the six authentic compilers), in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly states 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' (wala' is for the one who frees). The author analyzes the Arabic phrasing, noting that 'al-wala'' with the definite article indicates the genus, and 'liman a'taqa' with the lam of specification shows exclusivity — just as in 'al-hamdu lillah', all praise is exclusively for Allah. The passage then lists a wide consensus of Companions, Tabi'un, and Imams (including Malik, Shafi'i, and the Hanafis) who hold this view. Shuraih is quoted comparing wala' to inherited wealth. The discussion then turns to the two legal effects of wala': (1) the emancipator inherits from the freed slave if the latter dies without any Qur'anic heirs, and (2) the emancipator (along with his clan) is responsible for paying the blood money (aqila/diya) if the freed slave commits accidental homicide. The author explains these effects through two proofs: mutual support (al-tanassur) and the principle that liability is in exchange for liability (al-ghurm bi al-ghurm). Further, the author uses the analogy of revival: slavery is likened to figurative death, and emancipation to giving life, so the emancipator becomes like a father who gives life to a child, thus inheriting. The passage concludes with a question: if the emancipator dies without 'asaba, should the freed slave inherit from him? The answer is no, because the freed slave is a stranger to the emancipator, and the rule of wala' is a text-based exception contrary to qiyas; it cannot be extended analogically.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā’): The bond of clientage that arises from emancipation, giving the emancipator right to inherit the freed slave and responsibility for his blood money.
العقل (al-‘aql): Blood money (diya) paid by the emancipator and his clan for accidental killing by the freed slave.
الغرم بالغرم (al-ghurm bi al-ghurm): A legal maxim meaning that liability is in exchange for liability; used here to justify the emancipator's inheritance in return for his financial responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master frees a slave and later the slave dies without any qur'anic heirs, who inherits from the freed slave?
A) The state treasury (bayt al-mal)
B) The emancipator, due to wala'
C) The freed slave's former owners
D) The emancipator's own heirs

Key Takeaway
Wala' is a fixed bond of clientage that belongs exclusively to the emancipator, carrying the effects of inheritance and blood-money responsibility, and cannot be transferred or sold.

Ruling on the Condition of Sa'ibah in Emancipation
Ruling that the condition of sa'ibah is invalid and wala' belongs to the emancipator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the condition of sa'ibah in emancipation and apply the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' to invalidate any condition that contradicts it.

Core Idea
The condition of sa'ibah contradicts the hadith; wala' always belongs to the emancipator.

When a slave is emancipated, the default ruling is that the emancipator retains wala' (the right of inheritance and responsibility for diyah). But what if the emancipator stipulates that the freed slave is sa'ibah—meaning no one has wala' over him? This lesson examines the Hanafi position: such a condition is void, and wala' remains with the emancipator, based on the explicit text of the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The act is emancipation (itq), and the condition is that the slave becomes sa'ibah (no wala' over him). Recognize that the condition attempts to alter the default rule of wala'.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on the condition
The Hanafi school holds that the condition is batil (void) because it contradicts the hadith. The wala' therefore belongs to the emancipator, as if the condition had never been made.
3
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' (wala' belongs to the one who emancipates). This is a general text (amm) that covers all emancipators, and no exception is made for sa'ibah.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'li man a'taqa' is general. Any condition that attempts to negate the wala' from the emancipator is a contradiction to this Nass. The principle is that a condition contrary to the text of the Shari'ah is invalid.
In Hanafi usul, if a condition contradicts a Nass, it is void.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik held that the condition is valid and wala' goes to the Muslim community. The Hanafi response, as stated in the text, is simply that the condition is contrary to the Nass and therefore invalid. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that inheritance and diyah go to the emancipator in the Hanafi school, whereas in the Maliki school they go to the Muslim treasury.

Source Text

قال فان شرط انه سائبة فالشرط باطل والولاء لمن اعتق لان الشرط مخالف للنص فلا يصح تشریح قوله فان شرط الخ - جب سابق میں یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ ولاء آزاد کنندہ کو ملے گی ۔ تو اب اگر اعتاق میں یہ شرط ہو کہ یہ مملوک سائبہ ہو گا۔ یعنی آزادی کے بعد کسی کی ولاء میں نہ ہوگا بلکہ وہ خود مختار ہو گا جہاں چاہے جائے اور جو چاہے کرے۔ بان قال: لا ولاء لا حد عليك انت سائبة، قد اعتقتك سائبة، انت حر سائبة وغيره توحسن بصرى ابن سیرین، ابراهیم شفی شعمی ، راشد بن سعد ضمر و بن حبیب، اہل عرب، امام شافعی اور احناف وغیرہ جمہور علماء کے نزدیک شرط مذکور باطل ہوگی اور ولاء اسی کو ملے گی جس نے آزاد کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ شرط مذکور نص حدیث کے خلاف ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف عمر بن عبد العزیز ، زہری ہلکحول، ابو العالیہ اور امام مالک کے نزدیک اس کی ولاء جماعت مسلمین کے لئے ہوگی پس وہ اس کے وارث ہوں گے۔ اور وہ اس کی طرف سے دیت کا بوجھ اٹھا ئیں گے بنا یہ میں ہے: وفى المنصوص عن احمد لو خلف مالا ولم يدع وارثا اشترى بماله رقاباً فاعتقهم
He said: If he stipulates that he is sa'ibah, the stipulation is void and the wala' belongs to the one who emancipated, because the stipulation contradicts the text and is not valid. Commentary on his statement 'If he stipulates...' - When it was previously established that wala' goes to the emancipator, now if in the emancipation there is this stipulation that the slave will be sa'ibah, i.e., after freedom he will not be under anyone's wala' but will be independent, going wherever he wishes and doing whatever he wishes, by saying: 'There is no wala' for anyone upon you, you are sa'ibah,' 'I have freed you as sa'ibah,' 'You are free as sa'ibah,' etc., then according to Hasan al-Basri, Ibn Sirin, Ibrahim al-Shafi'i? [al-Nakha'i?], Rashid ibn Sa'd, Damrah ibn Habib, the people of Arabia, Imam al-Shafi'i, and the Hanafis and the majority of scholars, the mentioned stipulation is void and wala' will go to the one who freed, because the mentioned stipulation is contrary to the textual hadith. Contrary to this, according to 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, al-Zuhri, Mak-hul, Abu al-'Aliyah, and Imam Malik, its wala' will be for the Muslim community, so they will inherit from him and bear the burden of diyah on his behalf. Al-Binayah has: And in the transmitted text from Ahmad: If he leaves wealth and no heir, with his wealth slaves are purchased and freed.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the condition of sa'ibah in emancipation. The author begins by quoting the matn: 'If he stipulates that the slave is sa'ibah, the stipulation is void and the wala' belongs to the one who emancipated, because the stipulation contradicts the text (hadith) and is therefore invalid.' The commentary then explains the views of various scholars, noting that the majority (including Imam Shafi'i and the Hanafis) hold that the stipulation is void and wala' remains with the emancipator, while a minority (including Imam Malik) hold that wala' goes to the Muslim community. The Hanafi position is based on the general application of the hadith 'al-wala' liman a'taqa' (wala' belongs to the emancipator), and any condition that contradicts this Nass is null and void. The practical consequence is that the emancipator retains the rights of inheritance and payment of diyah from the freed slave.

Key Terms
سائبة (sā'ibah): A slave freed with the condition that no one has wala' over him; he is independent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person frees a slave with the condition that the slave is sa'ibah (no wala' to anyone), what is the ruling on the wala'?
A) Wala' goes to the Muslim community.
B) Wala' belongs to the emancipator.
C) Wala' is nullified.
D) The slave chooses his own master.

Key Takeaway
The condition of sa'ibah in emancipation is void, and wala' always belongs to the emancipator because it contradicts the definitive hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa'.

Wala' for the Emancipator in Special Cases
Rulings on wala' for makatib, mudabbir, umm walad, and mahram ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that wala' belongs to the master who caused emancipation, even if freedom occurs after the master's death, and apply it to cases of mukatib, mudabbir, umm walad, and mahram ownership.

Core Idea
Wala' follows the cause of emancipation, not the timing of freedom.

In this lesson, we examine several scenarios from al-Hidayah where a slave becomes free after the master's death, yet the wala' still belongs to the master. This seemingly counterintuitive rule is based on a fundamental principle: the master's action that set the cause of emancipation (such as kitabah, tadbir, or istilad) is sufficient to establish wala', even if the actual freedom occurs posthumously. Understanding this principle will help you navigate similar cases and avoid the common misconception that wala' is nullified by the master's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each scenario
Read each case (mukatib, wasiyya, mudabbir/umm walad, mahram) and note that in each, the slave is freed after the master's death but wala' goes to the master.
The matn explicitly states the ruling for each.
2
Extract the common cause (sabab)
For each scenario, identify what action the master performed that set the cause of emancipation. Common causes: kitabah (contract), tadbir (declaration), istilad (bearing a child), wasiyya (will), or ownership of mahram.
The text says 'because he is freed upon him by the cause that he undertook'.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Understand that the master's causative act is treated as if he performed the emancipation himself, so wala' attaches to him even if freedom occurs after his death.
This is analogous to a direct emancipation during lifetime.
4
Apply to the apostasy scenario
Consider the commentary's hypothetical: a master apostatizes, is declared dead, then returns Muslim. The mudabbir/umm walad freed in between dies; wala' still goes to the master because emancipation was caused by his earlier tadbir/istilad.
The cause remains even if the master's status changes temporarily.

He said: And when the mukatib pays, he is freed and the wala' is for the master, even if he is freed after the master's death, because he is freed upon him [the master] by the cause that he undertook, which is kitabah, and we have explained it in the book of mukatib. Likewise, the slave for whose emancipation or purchase and emancipation was willed, after his [the testator's] death, because the action of the executor after his death is like his action, and the estate is under the rule of his ownership. And if the master dies, his mudabbirs and ummahat al-awlad are freed, as we have explained in the [book of] emancipation, and their wala' is for him, because he freed them by tadbir and istilad. And whoever owns a mahram relative, he is freed upon him, as we have explained in emancipation, and his wala' is for him, because of the existence of the cause, which is emancipation upon him. His statement 'And when the mukatib pays...' etc. - the whole expression is clear from the translation and does not need commentary. However, regarding 'And if the master dies, his mudabbirs and ummahat al-awlad are freed', this question arises: The mudabbir and umm walad become free after the master's death, so how is it plausible that the master gets their wala'? The answer: If the master apostatized and went to dar al-harb (God forbid), and the qadi ruled his death and decreed the freedom of his mudabbir and ummahat al-awlad, then later the master returned as a Muslim and the mudabbir or umm walad died, then his wala' goes to the master.

Commentary
This passage covers several scenarios where wala' belongs to the emancipator even though emancipation occurs after the emancipator's death. The matn lists: a mukatib who completes payment after the master's death, a slave for whose emancipation was willed, mudabbirs and umm walads who become free upon the master's death, and ownership of a mahram relative which triggers automatic emancipation. In all cases, the wala' goes to the original master because the emancipation was caused by his actions (kitabah, tadbir, istilad, or ownership of mahram). The commentary then addresses a specific question: if a master becomes an apostate and flees to dar al-harb, and the qadi declares him dead, freeing his mudabbir and umm walad, then later the master returns as a Muslim and the mudabbir or umm walad dies, who gets the wala'? The answer is that the wala' still goes to the master, because the emancipation was due to his earlier actions.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master to pay a sum for his freedom.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been told by his master 'you are free after my death'.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child for her master.
ولاء (walā'): Clientage; the bond of inheritance and responsibility between an emancipator and a freed slave.

A) The mukatib himself.
B) The master's heirs as a group.
C) The master (deceased) - it goes to his estate.
D) No one; wala' is nullified.

Key Takeaway
Wala' is established by the master's causative act of emancipation, regardless of whether freedom occurs before or after his death.

Wala' of a Child Born to a Freed Pregnant Slave Woman
Ruling with talil on wala' of child from freed pregnant slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wala' of a child born to a freed pregnant slave woman and apply the six-month rule to determine whether the child is freed directly or as a follower.

Core Idea
The wala' of a child freed as part of its mother belongs to the mother's master and never transfers.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a slave woman is freed while pregnant from a slave husband. What happens to the child's wala'? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on the principle that the child is a part of the mother, so its freedom and wala' follow hers. The passage also clarifies the impact of the timing of birth and distinguishes a related case where a pregnant woman marries a free man.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core case and ruling
The passage begins with the case: a slave woman married to a slave is freed while pregnant. The ruling: both mother and child are freed, and the child's wala' belongs to the mother's master permanently.
This is the baseline rule.
2
Understand the talil (rational proof)
The reasoning (talil) is that the child is a part (juz') of the mother and accepts emancipation as part of her (taqabbala al-'itq maqsudan). Therefore, its wala' follows hers and does not transfer to the father's master.
The Hanafi reliance on the concept of 'juz'iyya' is key.
3
Apply the six-month rule for determination of certainty
If the child is born within six months of emancipation, it is certain that it existed at the time of emancipation, so it is freed directly (maqsudan). If born after six months, it is freed as a follower (taba'an) because its existence was not certain at the time of emancipation. In both cases, wala' goes to the mother's master.
The six-month period is based on the minimum duration of pregnancy.
4
Note the exception for twins
If twins are born and one is born within six months, both are considered to have been present at the time of emancipation (because they are twins connected together), so both are freed directly.
This is a special rule based on simultaneity.
5
Contrast with the marriage scenario
The passage contrasts with the case where a pregnant woman marries (not a freed slave) and the husband is a client of another master. In that case, the child's wala' goes to the father's master because the child is not considered part of the mother's contract; it becomes free through the father's status.
The principle differs: here the child is not freed as part of the mother's emancipation.

Source Text

And if a slave of a man marries a slave woman of another, then the master of the slave woman frees her while she is pregnant from the slave, she is freed and her pregnancy is freed, and the wala' of the pregnancy belongs to the master of the mother, never transferring from him, because it [the child] was freed upon the liberator of the mother intentionally, as it is a part of her that can be freed intentionally, so its wala' does not transfer from him, acting upon what we reported. And similarly, if she gives birth to a child in less than six months, because of certainty of the existence of the pregnancy at the time of emancipation, or if she gives birth to two children, one of them in less than six months, because they are twins and are connected together. And this is contrary to the case where she marries a man while she is pregnant, and the husband is a client of someone else: there the wala' of the child is for the master of the father, because the fetus is not capable of this wala' intentionally, because its completion is through offer and acceptance, and it is not a locus for it. He said: If she gives birth after her emancipation after more than six months, a child, then his wala' is for the masters of the mother, because he was freed as a follower of the mother, due to his connection to her after her emancipation, so he follows her in wala', and it was not certain of his existence at the time of emancipation such that he would be freed intentionally. Translation: If a slave of someone marries a slave woman of another, then the master of the slave woman frees her while she is pregnant from the slave, she is freed and her pregnancy is freed, and the wala' of the pregnancy belongs to the master of the mother, it will never transfer from him, because
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage deals with the wala' of a child born to a slave woman who is freed while pregnant. The matn states: If a slave married another slave woman and then the woman's master frees her while she is pregnant from the slave, she is freed and her child is also freed, and the wala' of the child belongs to the mother's master and never transfers. This is because the child is a part of the mother and was emancipated as a dependent. The child was intended to be freed as part of her. Then it discusses cases based on the time of birth: if born within six months of emancipation, it is certain the pregnancy existed at the time of emancipation, so the child is freed directly. If born after six months, the child is freed as a follower (taba') of the mother, but the wala' still goes to the mother's master because the child was not certainly in existence at the time of emancipation. Contrast with the case of a woman who marries while pregnant; there the child's wala' goes to the father's master because the child is not considered part of the mother's sale.

Key Terms
حمل (ḥaml): The fetus in the womb; legally a part of the mother until birth.
ولاء (walā'): The legal bond of patronage between the emancipator and the freed person, including inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mother's master.
B) The father's master.
C) The child is free with no wala'.
D) The Muslim community.

Key Takeaway
The wala' of a child born to a freed pregnant slave woman permanently belongs to the mother's master, whether the child is freed directly or as a follower.

Invalidity of Sa'iba Condition in Emancipation
Inter-madhhab dispute on sa'iba condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sa'iba condition, explain the dalil from hadith, and contrast the Maliki position with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Wala' is fixed by the cause of emancipation; any condition negating it is invalid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the bond of wala' (clientage) is an automatic consequence of emancipation. Can the emancipator stipulate that the freed slave has no master? This passage addresses the inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of such a condition (sa'iba). The Hanafi school, along with the majority, holds that the condition is void and wala' belongs to the emancipator, relying on the hadith that establishes wala' for the one who sets free. The Maliki school differs, assigning wala' to the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on sa'iba
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding a condition that the freed slave is sa'iba (no master). The ruling is that the condition is batil (invalid) and wala' remains with the emancipator.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
List the scholars who hold a different view: Imam Malik, Umar bin Abd al-Aziz, Zuhri, Mak-hul, Abu al-Aliyah. Their position is that wala' goes to the Muslim community (bayt al-mal).
The passage does not detail their dalil, but it may be based on general permissibility of stipulations.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing view
The Hanafi response is that the condition contradicts the explicit text of hadith, so it is void. The hadith establishes wala' for the emancipator unconditionally.
This shows that Hanafi usul gives priority to explicit textual evidence over stipulations.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the Hanafi view, the emancipator inherits from the freed slave and is liable for blood money. In the Maliki view, the Muslim community inherits and bears the liability.
This difference affects estate distribution and diyah obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الولاء
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۲
جائے وہ اس پر آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اس کی ولاء اس کے لئے ہوگی۔ وجو د سبب کی وجہ سے اور وہ اس پر آزاد ہونا ہے۔ تشریح قوله فان شرط الخ - جب سابق میں یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ ولاء آزاد کنندہ کو ملے گی ۔ تو اب اگر اعتاق میں یہ شرط ہو کہ یہ مملوک سائبہ ہو گا۔ یعنی آزادی کے بعد کسی کی ولاء میں نہ ہوگا بلکہ وہ خود مختار ہو گا جہاں چاہے جائے اور جو چاہے کرے۔ بان قال: لا ولاء لا حد عليك انت سائبة، قد اعتقتك سائبة، انت حر سائبة وغيره توحسن بصرى ابن سیرین، ابراهیم شفی شعمی ، راشد بن سعد ضمر و بن حبیب، اہل عرب، امام شافعی اور احناف وغیرہ جمہور علماء کے نزدیک شرط مذکور باطل ہوگی اور ولاء اسی کو ملے گی جس نے آزاد کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ شرط مذکور نص حدیث کے خلاف ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف عمر بن عبد العزیز ، زہری ہلکحول، ابو العالیہ اور امام مالک کے نزدیک اس کی ولاء جماعت مسلمین کے لئے ہوگی پس وہ اس کے وارث ہوں گے۔ اور وہ اس کی طرف سے دیت کا بوجھ اٹھا ئیں گے بنا یہ میں ہے:
وفى المنصوص عن احمد لو خلف مالا ولم يدع وارثا اشترى بماله رقاباً فاعتقهم
Book of Wala'. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 12. He will become free upon it, and his wala' will be for him, due to the existence of the cause, and that is his being freed upon it. Explanation of his statement: 'For if he stipulates etc.' - When it was established previously that wala' belongs to the emancipator, now if in the emancipation there is a condition that this slave will be sa'iba, i.e., after freedom he will have no wala' over anyone, but will be independent, going wherever he wishes and doing whatever he wishes, such as saying: 'No wala' for anyone over you, you are sa'iba,' 'I have emancipated you as sa'iba,' 'You are free, sa'iba,' etc. According to Hasan of Basra, Ibn Sirin, Ibrahim, Shafi'i, Shami, Rashid bin Sa'd, Damrah bin Habib, the people of Arabia, Imam Shafi'i, the Hanafis, and the majority of scholars, this condition is invalid, and wala' will belong to the one who emancipated, because this condition contradicts the text of hadith. Contrary to this, according to Umar bin Abd al-Aziz, Zuhri, Mak-hul, Abu al-Aliyah, and Imam Malik, his wala' will be for the Muslim community, so they will inherit from him, and they will bear the burden of blood money for him. Based on this, it is narrated from Ahmad: 'If he leaves wealth and no heir, his wealth is used to buy slaves and set them free.'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses the inter-madhhab dispute regarding the condition of sa'iba (a slave freed with the stipulation that he has no master). The Hanafi school, along with the majority of scholars including Imam Shafi'i, holds that such a condition is invalid and that wala' (clientage) remains with the emancipator. The proof is the hadith which states that wala' belongs to the one who sets free, and any condition contradicting this is null. The opposing view, attributed to Imam Malik and some early scholars, is that wala' goes to the Muslim community (bayt al-mal), who inherit from the freed slave and are liable for his blood money. The text also mentions a narration from Imam Ahmad illustrating a similar principle. The Hanafi position is considered the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Key Terms
سائبة (sa'iba): A slave freed with the condition that he has no master (wala') over him.
ولاء (wala'): The bond of clientage established upon emancipation, giving the former master the right to inherit from the freed slave and liability for blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is free and has no wala' over him.
B) The condition is invalid and wala' belongs to the emancipator.
C) The wala' goes to the Muslim community.
D) The slave remains a slave until the condition is removed.

Key Takeaway
The condition of sa'iba is invalid in Hanafi fiqh; wala' remains with the emancipator because it is fixed by the cause of emancipation and cannot be negated by stipulation.

Wala' of Mudabbar and Umm Walad After Master's Apostasy and Return
Ruling on wala' of mudabbar/umm walad after master's apostasy and return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wala' of a mudabbar or umm walad when the master apostatizes, is declared dead by a qadi, and later returns as a Muslim.

Core Idea
A master's return after apostasy revives his wala' rights over mudabbar and umm walad, even if a qadi had declared him dead.

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced scenario involving mudabbar and umm walad emancipation. Normally, these slaves are freed upon the master's actual death. But what if the master apostatizes and a qadi declares him dead, freeing them prematurely? Does the master lose all rights if he later repents and returns? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer that preserves the master's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the normal emancipation rule
Mudabbar and umm walad become free upon the actual death of their master. Their wala' then goes to the master's heirs.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Identify the exceptional scenario
If the master apostatizes and flees to dar al-harb, the qadi may declare him dead and decree the freedom of his mudabbar and umm walad.
This is a judicial death, not actual.
3
Consider the master's return
If the master repents and returns as a Muslim, his rights are revived because the qadi's declaration was based on presumed death.
Return must be as a Muslim.
4
Apply the ruling
If the mudabbar or umm walad dies after the master's return, their wala' goes to the master, not to the community or heirs.
The master inherits the wala' as if no emancipation had occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا ادى ..... الخ - يركل عبارت ترجمہ سے واضح ہے محتاج تشریح نہیں ہے البتہ وان مـات الـمـولـى عتق مدبر وہ وامهات اولاده “ پر یہ سوال ہوتا ہے کہ مدبر اور ام ولد اپنے آقا کی موت کے بعد آزاد ہوتے ہیں تو آقا کو ان کی ولاء ملنے کی کون سی صور بھی وجاہت ہے کہ آقا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب چلا گیا ( العیاذ باللہ ) اور قاضی نے اس کی موت کا حکم کر کے اس کے مدبر اور اس کی امہات الاولاد کی آزادی کا فیصلہ کر دیا اس کے بعد آقا مسلمان ہو کر واپس چلا آیا اور مدبر یا ام ولد مرگئی ۔ تو اس کی ولاء آقا کو ملے گی۔
His statement: 'And if he performs... etc.' - The translation of the phrase is clear and needs no explanation. However, regarding the statement 'And if the master dies, the mudabbar and umm walad become free,' a question arises: Mudabbar and umm walad become free after the death of their master, so what scenario is there for the master to get their wala'? The scenario is if the master apostatizes and goes to dar al-harb (God forbid), and the qadi declares his death and issues a ruling freeing his mudabbar and umm walad, then after that the master returns as a Muslim, and the mudabbar or umm walad dies. Then his wala' goes to the master.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage clarifies a specific scenario regarding mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is postponed until the master's death) and umm walad (a slave woman who bore her master's child, who becomes free at his death). Normally, they are freed upon the master's actual death. However, if the master apostatizes and goes to enemy territory, a qadi may declare him dead and decree their freedom. If the master later repents and returns, and then the mudabbar or umm walad dies, their wala' goes to the master, because the original emancipation was based on a judicial death that was not actual. The master's return revives his rights.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is contingent upon the master's death.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears her master's child, becoming free upon his death.

Checkpoint

A) The mudabbar and umm walad remain free and their wala' goes to the Muslim community.

Wala' of Child When Mother Freed Pregnant
Hanafi ruling on wala' of child when mother freed pregnant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wala' of a child born to a freed pregnant slave woman and apply the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
The child of a freed pregnant slave is freed with her, and his wala' belongs exclusively to the mother’s master.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies a nuanced issue of wala' (clientage) when a pregnant slave woman is freed. The child is considered a part of the mother, so emancipation covers the fetus as well. The wala' of the child thus follows that of the mother, based on the well-known hadith. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between wala' by emancipation and wala' by contract (muwalat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Read the scenario: a slave man marries a slave woman of another master, then the woman's master frees her while she is pregnant from the slave. State the parties involved.
The father remains a slave; only the mother is freed.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Extract the ruling: the mother and her unborn child become free, and the child's wala' belongs to the mother's former master (the emancipator), never to be transferred.
3
Locate the primary dalil
Identify the proof: the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' (wala' belongs to the one who sets free). Note its status as a hadith ahad and its authority (zanni al-thubut).
The text references it as 'بما روينا' (by what we narrated).
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith applies: since the child was freed by the same act of emancipation (as part of the mother), the mother's master is effectively his emancipator. Therefore, his wala' belongs to that master.
The child is 'جذء منها' (a part of her) and 'يقبل الاعتاق مقصودا' (capable of being the direct object of emancipation).
5
Distinguish from muwalat
Note the contrast: if the mother entered a muwalat contract while pregnant, the fetus cannot be a party to it, so the child's wala' follows the father's muwali. This shows the difference between wala' by emancipation and wala' by contract.
Muwalat requires offer and acceptance; a fetus cannot participate.
