ولنا قوله تعالى والذين عقدت ايمانكم فاتوهم نصيبهم والآية في الموالاة
ترجمہ ہماری دلیل حق تعالیٰ کا ارشاد ہے اور جن سے معاہدہ ہوا تمہارا ان کو دے دو ان کا حصہ یہ آیت دربارہ موالات نازل ہوئی ہے۔
تشریح قوله ولنا قوله تعالى ... الخ - ہماری دلیل یہ آیت ہے "والذین عقدت ایمانکم فاتوهم نصيبهم" اس میں عقدت ايمانكم بمعنی عاقد تم ہے۔ جیسے دوسری آیت میں ہے۔ یوم ينظر المرء ما قدمت بداه ای نفسه چونکه کسب و عمل اکثر اوقات ہاتھوں سے ہوتا ہے اس لئے عقد کی اضافت ایمان کی طرف کر دی گئی۔
وجہ استدلال یہ ہے کہ یہ آیت عقد موالات کے بارے میں نازل ہوئی ہے۔ بدلیل آنکہ سابق آیت "ولكل جعلنا موالى مما ترك الوالدان والاقربون" میں ارث کے طور پر استحقاق نصیب کا بیان مقصود تھا نہ کہ ابتداء قرابت اور برزو نیکی کے طور پر تو ایسے ہی جو اس پر معطوف ہے۔ اس سے مراد بھی استحقاق بطریق ارث ہی ہوگا کیونکہ معطوف و معطوف علیہ کا حکم ایک ہوتا ہے مبسوط بکری میں ہے کہ حق تعالیٰ نے ولاء موالات کو علی الطلاق توارث کا سبب قرار دیا ہے۔ اس فرق کے بغیر کہ میت کے لئے وارث آخر ہو یا نہ ہو۔ صرف اتنی بات ہے کہ جس کا کوئی دوسرا وارث ہو اس کے حق میں یہ قول باری "و اولوالارحام بعضهم اولی ببعض" سے منسوخ ہو گیا اور جس کا کوئی اور وارث نہ ہو اس کے حق میں کوئی ناسخ نہیں ہے تو وہ ظاہر آیت کے تحت میں داخل رہے گا۔
دوسری دلیل
وسئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم عن رجل اسلم على يد رجل آخر ووالاه فقال هو احق الناس به محياه ومماته وهذا يشير الى العقل والارث في حالتين هاتين ولان ماله حقه فيصرفه الى حيث يشاء والصرف الى بيت المال ضرورة عدم المستحق لا انـه مستحق
ترجمہ اور آنحضرت ﷺ سے اس شخص کی بابت دریافت کیا گیا جو کسی دوسرے کے ہاتھ پر اسلام لایا اور اس نے اس سے موالات کی تو آپ نے فرمایا اس کی زندگی وموت میں سب لوگوں سے زیادہ وہی حقدار ہے اور یہ مشیر ہے ان دونوں حالتوں میں عاقلہ ہونے اور میراث کی طرف اور اس لئے کہ اس کا مال اسی کا حق ہے تو وہ جہاں چاہے صرف کرے اور بیت المال کی جانب صرف کرنا تو مستحق نہ ہونے کی ضرورت سے ہوتا ہے نہ اس لئے کہ بیت المال اس کا مستحق ہے۔
تشریح قوله وسئل .. الخ ہماری دوسری دلیل حضرت تمیم داری کی حدیث ہے
قال: يا رسول الله : ما السنة في الرحل يسلم على يد رجل من المسلمين ؟ قال: هو اولى الناس بمحياه ومماته (اصحاب سنن اربعہ، حاکم احمد، ابن ابی شیبه، دارمی، ابویعلی موصلی، طبرانی دار قطنی، عبدالرزاق، بخاری الفرائض تعلیقا۔ واللفظ لابی داؤد) صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ حدیث موت و حیات دونوں حالتوں کے ذکر سے عقل و میراث کی طرف مشیر ہے۔ یعنی حالت حیات میں عقل دیت کی طرف اور موت کے بعد میراث کی طرف۔ تنبیه حدیث مذکور میں دو اعتبار سے کلام کیا گیا ہے۔ اول باعتبار اتصال و انقطاع۔ دوم باعتبار ضعف اسناد، اول کی بابت امام ترندی نے کہا ہے :
The Book of Wala. First, because wala' of manumission cannot be transferred in any state, unlike wala' of allegiance where the master has the right to have it transferred before payment of blood-wit. Second, because wala' of manumission involves a legal revitalization, unlike wala' of allegiance where there is no revitalization. Third... because regarding wala' of manumission being a cause of inheritance and taking precedence over uterine relatives, there is consensus, unlike wala' of allegiance where there is disagreement among the imams. Thus Imam al-Shafi'i does not accept wala' of allegiance. He says that wala' is only wala' of manumission. The three imams also adopted this. Then the linguistic and technical meanings of wala' have already passed at the beginning of the Book of Wala. As for the specific interpretation of wala' of allegiance, in Sawad al-Dhakhirah etc. it is written: 'It is that a man accepts Islam at the hand of another man, and he says to the one at whose hand he accepted Islam or to someone else: "I have made a contract of allegiance with you on condition that if I die, my inheritance goes to you, and if I commit a crime, the blood-wit is upon you and your 'aqilah," and the other accepts.' That is, wala' of allegiance is that a person accepts Islam at the hand of someone else, and he says to him or to another: 'I have made allegiance with you on condition that if I die, my inheritance will be yours, and if I commit a crime, its blood-wit is upon your 'aqilah,' and the other person accepts it. His statement: 'And when he accepts Islam...' etc. A person accepts Islam at the hand of another, and the new Muslim makes a contract of allegiance with him that after his death he will be his sole heir, and if he commits some offense, he will pay the blood-wit on his behalf; or he accepts Islam at the hand of someone else and also makes allegiance with another Muslim. According to us, this contract is valid. Now after the death of this new Muslim, that person will be his heir if he has no other heir, and in case of a crime he will pay compensation on his behalf. This is narrated from 'Umar, 'Uthman, and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them) that they permitted allegiance and caused inheritance through it. Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, al-Hasan al-Basri, Hakam, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, Rabi'ah, Yahya ibn Sa'id, Layth ibn Sa'd, 'Attar al-Zuhri, Hakam, Hammad, Imam Abu Hanifah and his companions are of this opinion. His statement: 'And al-Shafi'i said...' etc. According to the three imams and Imam al-Shafi'i, allegiance is nothing (i.e., it is not something that causes inheritance and blood-wit) because it necessitates the nullification of the right of the public treasury, since inheritance is based on kinship or marriage, and according to the hadith of 'Itq, on [wala' of] manumission. And here none of these found. And in the case of no heir, the wealth goes to the public treasury. So if the contract of allegiance is considered valid, the right of the public treasury would be eliminated, whereas observance of the right of the public treasury is necessary. Due to this observance of the right of the public treasury, according to Imam al-Shafi'i, bequeathing all wealth is not permissible even if the testator has no heir; rather only one-third of the wealth is permissible. Also, for this reason, this allegiance does not take effect in the presence of another heir. Thus it is not valid as against the public treasury. The Hanafis' proof: And our proof is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And those with whom your right hands have made a contract, give them their share.' And the verse concerns allegiance. Translation: Our proof is the command of Allah the Exalted: 'And those with whom you have made a contract, give them their share.' This verse was revealed regarding allegiance. Explanation of his statement: 'And our proof is the saying of Allah...' etc. Our proof is this verse: 'And those with whom your right hands have made a contract' — here 'aqadt aymanukum means 'you have made a contract' just as in another verse: 'On the day when man looks at what his hands have sent forth,' i.e., his self, because earning and actions are mostly done by hands, so the contract is attributed to hands. The point of inference is that this verse was revealed concerning the contract of allegiance, as evidence that the preceding verse 'And for each We have made clients of what parents and near relatives leave' intended the entitlement of a share by way of inheritance, not by way of beginning kinship or goodness. So similarly, that which is coordinated with it also means entitlement by way of inheritance, because the coordinate and the coordinated have the same ruling. In al-Mabsut it is stated that Allah the Exalted made wala' of allegiance an absolute cause of inheritance without distinction as to whether the deceased has any other heir. The only point is that for one who has another heir, this saying of Allah 'and those related by blood are more entitled to each other' abrogated it, and for one who has no other heir, there is no abrogator, so it remains under the apparent meaning of the verse. The second proof: And the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) was asked about a man who accepted Islam at the hand of another man and made allegiance with him. He said: 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and after his death.' And this indicates blood-wit and inheritance in these two states. And because his wealth is his own right, so he may dispose of it as he wishes. And the disposal to the public treasury is due to the necessity of the absence of a claimant, not because the public treasury is entitled to it. Translation: And the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about a person who accepted Islam at the hand of another and made allegiance with him. He said: 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and death.' And this indicates the blood-wit and inheritance in both states. And because his wealth is his right, he may spend it wherever he wishes. And spending to the public treasury is due to the necessity of the absence of a claimant, not because the public treasury is entitled to it. Explanation of his statement: 'And he was asked...' etc. Our second proof is the hadith of Tamim al-Dari: He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is the Sunnah concerning a man who accepts Islam at the hand of a Muslim?' He said: 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and after his death.' (Recorded by the four Sunan compilers, al-Hakim, Ahmad, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Darimi, Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, 'Abd al-Razzaq, and al-Bukhari in al-Fara'id in a suspended form. The wording is of Abu Dawud.) The author of al-Hidayah says that this hadith indicates blood-wit and inheritance by mentioning both states of life and death, i.e., in life it indicates blood-wit, and after death inheritance. Note: This hadith has been discussed from two aspects: first, regarding its continuity and discontinuity; second, regarding weakness of its chain. Regarding the first, Imam al-Tirmidhi said...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat (clientage by allegiance). It begins by distinguishing Wala' al-Muwalat from Wala' al-'Itaqah (clientage by manumission) in three respects: (1) Wala' al-'Itaqah cannot be transferred, while Wala' al-Muwalat can, as the master has the right to transfer the obligation of blood-wit before payment. (2) Wala' al-'Itaqah involves the legal revitalization (ihya') of the slave, which is absent in Wala' al-Muwalat. (3) There is consensus that Wala' al-'Itaqah' causes inheritance and takes precedence over uterine relatives, but there is disagreement regarding Wala' al-Muwalat. The author then defines Wala' al-Muwalat lexically and technically, quoting from Sawad al-Dhakhirah: it is a contract where a new Muslim says to the person at whose hand he accepted Islam (or another Muslim): 'I make a contract of allegiance with you on condition that if I die, my inheritance goes to you, and if I commit a crime, the blood-wit is upon you and your 'aqilah.' The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifah and his companions, is that this contract is valid and causes inheritance and blood-wit, supported by the practice of several Companions and Successors. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i and the three Imams (Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i himself) reject it entirely, arguing that inheritance is based on kinship, marriage, or wala' of manumission, and that accepting this contract would nullify the right of the public treasury (bayt al-mal) in cases where the deceased has no heir. They analogize this to their ruling that a person without heirs cannot bequeath more than one-third of his wealth. The Hanafis respond with two primary proofs. The first is the Quranic verse (4:33): 'And those with whom your right hands have made a contract, give them their share.' They argue that this verse was revealed concerning Wala' al-Muwalat and establishes it as a cause of inheritance, and that it is only abrogated for those who have other heirs (by the verse 'the relatives are more entitled'), but remains operative for those with no heirs. The second proof is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said about a man who accepted Islam and made allegiance: 'He is the most entitled of people to him in his life and after his death,' which the author of al-Hidayah interprets as indicating blood-wit during life and inheritance after death. The Hanafis also argue that the disposal of wealth to the public treasury is merely a necessity when no claimant exists, not because the treasury is entitled to it. The passage ends with a note that the hadith has been discussed regarding its continuity and chain weakness, referencing Imam al-Tirmidhi.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; a legal relationship of patronage and inheritance between a patron and a client, either through manumission or through contract.
موالات (muwalat): A contract of allegiance between a new Muslim and an existing Muslim, whereby the latter becomes the patron, inheriting from the former if he has no heirs and being responsible for his blood-wit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary Quranic proof for the validity of Wala' al-Muwalat?
A) The verse on bequests (2:180)
B) The verse 'And those with whom your right hands have made a contract, give them their share' (4:33)
C) The verse on inheritance (4:11)
D) The verse on manumission (90:13)

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-Muwalat is a valid contract in Hanafi law, supported by Quran and Hadith, but rejected by the Shafi'i school due to concerns over the public treasury.

intermediate
6 min
Defence of Wala al-Muwalat and Refutation of Objections
Defending the validity of wala al-muwalat against objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that wala al-muwalat is valid and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Specification by mention does not negate other forms: wala al-muwalat is valid despite the hadith on wala al-itaqa.

This passage defends the Hanafi position on wala al-muwalat (clientage by agreement) against objections from Imam al-Shafi'i and other critics. The commentator refutes claims that the supporting hadith is weak and provides additional proofs from hadith and the practice of the Companions. The key legal principle is that specification (takhsis) does not negate other categories.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: wala al-muwalat is valid and the mawla (the person at whose hands the convert accepted Islam) inherits from the convert if there are no other heirs.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that wala al-muwalat is invalid; inheritance goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal). His main proof is the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' (clientage belongs to the emancipator) and the claim that the hadith supporting wala al-muwalat is weak.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that the hadith 'man aslama ala yadaihi rajulun fa-wala'uhu lahu' is weak due to criticism of narrators Abd al-Aziz and Ibn Mawhab. Also, the general hadith on wala only mentions emancipation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The commentator defends the hadith by citing scholars like al-Hakim, Abu Zur'a, Ibn Hajar, and al-Dhahabi who authenticated the narrators. Additional narrations from Ummama, Amr ibn al-As, and Umar's practice are adduced. The reply to the 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' argument is that specification (takhsis) does not negate other forms of wala.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi – the convert's wealth goes to the person who brought him to Islam; Shafi'i – it goes to the public treasury if there are no agnatic heirs.

كتاب الولاء رہی ضعیف اسناد بات سو حافظ ابن المنذر نے کہا ہے ۔ "هذا الحديث مضطر و عبد العزيز را ويه ليس بالحافظ . خطابی کہتے ہیں " وقد ضعف احمد بن حنبل هذا الحديث وقال: ان راويه عبد العزيز ليس من اهل الحفظ والاتقان ۔ حاصل یہ کہ عبد العزیز اور عبد اللہ بن موہب دونوں کے بارے میں کلام کیا گیا ہے۔ سو جواب یہ ہے کہ حاکم نے اس حدیث کو بطریق ابن موہب عن تمیم روایت کرنے کے بعد کہا ہے صحیح علی شرط مسلم، شیخ ابو زرعہ دمشقی نے بھی اس کو صحیح مانا ہے اور کہا ہے ۔ ہو حدیث حسن اخرج متصل خود حافظ ابن حجر نے تقریب میں کہا کہ عبداللہ بن موہب طبقہ ثالثہ سے ہے اور ثقہ ہے صاحب کمال کہتے ہیں کہ ابن موہب کو عمر بن عبد العزیز نے قضاء فلسطین پر مامور کیا تھا اس سے عبدالعزیز ، زہری ، یزید بن عبداللہ عبد الملک بن ابی جمیلہ اور عمرو بن مہاجر راوی ہیں تو کیا ایسے راوی کو بھی مجہول کہتے ہیں؟ اسی لئے شیخ ذہبی کو کہنا پڑا۔ کہ اگر یحیی بن معین نے اس کو نہیں پہچانا تو کچھ مضر نہیں ۔ کیونکہ دوسروں نے اس کو ثقہ کہا ہے۔ رہا عبدالعزیز بن عمر کے حافظہ میں کلام سودہ اس لئے مقبول نہیں کہ یہ سیح بخاری کے رواۃ میں سے ہے شیخ ابوزرعہ نے کہا ہے لا باس بہ ، ابونعیم کہتے ہیں کہ یہ ثقہ ہے ، ابن عمار کا قول ہے۔ ثقہ لا اختلاف فیہ۔ یحیی بن معین فرماتے ہیں کہ ثقه روى يسراً فيما ذكرنا سقط اعتراض المعترضین کا لبيهقى والخطابي وابن القطان والشافعي فائدہ ... اس باب میں دیگر احادیث و آثار بھی ہیں۔ مثلاً 1) طبرانی نے معجم میں ، دار قطنی نے سنن میں اور ابن عدی نے الکامل میں بطریق معاویہ بن یحیی صدفی بواسطه قاسم بن عبد العزیز حضرت امامہ روایت کیا ہے" قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من اسلم على يديه رجل فولاء ة له "۔ ۲ نیز معجم طبرانی اور مسند ابن راہویہ میں حضرت عمرو بن العاص سے روایت ہے انه اتی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: ان رجلا اسلم على يده وله مال وقد مات قال: فلک میراثه اسی طرح مصنف ابن شیبہ میں مجاہد سے مروی ہے ان رجلا اتى عمر فقال ان رجلا اسلم على يدى فمات و ترک الف درهم فتحرجت منها ، فقال : ارايت لوجنى جناية على من تكون ؟ قال : على ، قال : فميراثه لك قوله ولان ماله حقه.... الخ یہ ہماری قیاسی دلیل ہے کہ اس کا مال تو اسی کا حق ہے تو اس کو اختیار ہے جہاں چاہے خرچ کرے۔ اور امام شافعی کا یہ کہنا لان فيه ابطال حق بیت المال “ اس لئے صحیح نہیں کہ بیت المال میں داخل کرنا تو اس ضرورت سے ہوتا ہے کہ کوئی مستحق موجود نہیں ہے نہ اس لئے کہ بیت المال کا استحقاق ہوتا ہے۔ سوال آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم کے ارشاد "الولاء لمن اعتق " سے تو یہی مفہوم ہوتا ہے ولاء موالات باطل ہے کیونکہ اس میں کوئی معتق نہیں ہے۔ جواب کسی مٹی کی تخصیص بالذکر ا سکے ماعدا کی نفی پر دال نہیں ہوتی ۔
Book of Wala. The remaining [hadith] is weak in chain. So Hafiz Ibn al-Mundhir said: 'This hadith is mudtarib and Abd al-Aziz, its narrator, is not a Hafiz.' Al-Khattabi says: 'Ahmad ibn Hanbal declared this hadith weak and said: Its narrator Abd al-Aziz is not from the people of memory and precision.' Summary: Both Abd al-Aziz and Abdullah ibn Mawhab have been criticized. So the answer is that al-Hakim narrated this hadith via Ibn Mawhab from Tamim and then said: 'Sahih according to the condition of Muslim.' Shaykh Abu Zur'a al-Dimashqi also considered it sahih and said it is a hasan hadith with a connected chain. Even Hafiz Ibn Hajar in al-Taqrib said that Abdullah ibn Mawhab is from the third level and is thiqah. Sahib Kamal says that Ibn Mawhab was appointed by Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz as judge of Palestine. Narrators from him include Abd al-Aziz, al-Zuhri, Yazid ibn Abdullah, Abd al-Malik ibn Abi Jamilah, and Amr ibn Muhajir. So how can such a narrator be called majhul? For this reason, Shaykh al-Dhahabi had to say: If Yahya ibn Ma'in did not recognize him, that is not harmful because others have declared him thiqah. As for the criticism of Abd al-Aziz ibn Umar's memory, it is not accepted because he is among the narrators of Sahih al-Bukhari. Shaykh Abu Zur'a said: 'No problem with him.' Abu Nu'aym says he is thiqah. Ibn Ammar says: 'Thiqah, no disagreement about him.' Yahya ibn Ma'in says: 'Thiqah, he narrated a few [hadith].' So the objection of the objectors falls, including al-Bayhaqi, al-Khattabi, Ibn al-Qattan, and al-Shafi'i. Benefit: In this chapter, there are other hadiths and athar. For example: 1) Al-Tabarani in al-Mu'jam, al-Daraqutni in al-Sunan, and Ibn Adi in al-Kamil via Muawiya ibn Yahya al-Sadafi through Qasim ibn Abd al-Aziz, from Ummama (may Allah be pleased with her) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever a man accepts Islam at his hands, his wala belongs to him.' 2) Also in al-Mu'jam al-Tabarani and Musnad Ibn Rahawayh from Amr ibn al-As that he came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and said: 'A man accepted Islam at my hands, he had wealth, and he died.' He said: 'You have his inheritance.' Similarly, in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba from Mujahid: 'A man came to Umar and said: A man accepted Islam at my hands, died, and left one thousand dirhams, and I am reluctant to take them. He said: If he had committed a crime, upon whom would it be? He said: Upon me. He said: Then his inheritance is yours.' His statement: 'And because his wealth is his right... etc. This is our analogical proof: his wealth is his right, so he has the choice to spend it wherever he wishes. And Imam al-Shafi'i's statement 'because it invalidates the right of the treasury' is not correct, because entering the treasury is only necessary when there is no other deserving person, not because the treasury has a substantive right. Question: From the Prophet's (peace be upon him) statement 'al-wala' li man a'taqa', it is understood that wala al-muwalat is invalid because there is no emancipator. Answer: Specification by mention does not negate other forms.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute defending the validity of wala al-muwalat (clientage by agreement) against objections, primarily from Imam al-Shafi'i and other hadith critics. The commentator first addresses the charge that the hadith supporting wala al-muwalat is weak. He rebuts by citing multiple scholars who authenticated the narrators, especially Abdullah ibn Mawhab and Abd al-Aziz ibn Umar, showing they are reliable. Then he brings additional narrations from the Companions and the practice of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) to support the ruling. The qiyas (analogy) argument is that since the wealth belongs to the person who brought the new Muslim, he has the right to inherit it. Al-Shafi'i's objection that this takes the right of the public treasury is answered: the treasury only receives property when there is no other rightful heir. Finally, the objection from the hadith 'al-wala' li man a'taqa' is refuted with the principle that specification (takhsis) by mention does not negate the existence of other types (i.e., wala al-muwalat is not negated by the hadith on wala al-itaqa). This follows the Hanafi position that wala al-muwalat is valid and distinct from wala al-itaqa.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالت (wala' al-muwalat): Clientage established by a contract of mutual alliance and inheritance between two non-related free Muslims, where one agrees to be the mawla of the other.
ولاء العتاقة (wala' al-itaqa): Clientage resulting from manumission of a slave, giving the emancipator inheritance rights.
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that the hadith supporting wala al-muwalat is weak?
A) They concede the hadith is weak but cite other proofs.
B) They rely on qiyas only.
C) They authenticate the hadith through multiple chains and narrators.
D) They agree with Imam al-Shafi'i that it is weak.

Key Takeaway
Wala al-muwalat is a valid form of clientage in the Hanafi school, supported by hadith and the practice of the Companions, and specification by mention does not negate other forms of wala.

Rulings of the Contract of Wala al-Muwalat
Explains the legal rulings of wala al-muwalat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions and binding nature of the contract of wala al-muwalat, and explain when it becomes irrevocable.

Core Idea
Wala al-muwalat is a contract of mutual patronage that is initially non-binding, but becomes binding once the mawla pays blood money.

This passage from Al-Hidayah sets out the key rulings of wala al-muwalat, a contract between a non-Arab convert and a patron. The Hanafi school recognizes this institution, while other schools do not. We will examine the conditions, the initial non-binding nature, and the point at which the contract becomes irrevocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and conditions
The contract is between a mawla (patron) and a non-Arab convert. Conditions: the mawla must not be Arab; the contract must include terms of inheritance and aqila (mutual liability for blood money).
The condition regarding the mawla not being Arab is because Arabs already have tribal support.
2
Understand the initial non-binding nature
Before any payment is made, the contract is like a bequest—non-binding. The mawla can transfer his wala to another, and the patron can disclaim it, but the patron's disclaimer must happen in the presence of the mawla (like intentional removal of an agent).
If the lower mawla contracts with another without the first patron's presence, it is a constructive annulment.
3
Recognize the constructive annulment
When the lower mawla contracts with a new patron without the first patron's knowledge, the first contract is annulled constructively (faskh hukmi), similar to the constructive removal of an agent in agency law.
4
Determine when it becomes binding
Once the mawla pays blood money (or any compensation) on behalf of the convert, the contract becomes binding. Neither the mawla nor his child can thereafter transfer the wala. Similarly, if he pays on behalf of his child, both are bound.
Payment creates a right of the other party, making the contract irrevocable.
5
Contrast with wala al-itaqa
A mawla who already has wala al-itaqa (from emancipation) cannot contract muwalat because his existing wala is binding and leaves no room for a lesser form.
Wala al-itaqa is stronger and prevails over muwalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و ان كان له وارث فهو اولى منه وان كانت عمةً أو خالة او غيرهما من ذوى الارحام لان الموالاة عقدهما فلا يلزم غيرهما وذو الرحم وارث ولابد من شرط الارث والعقل كما ذكر في الكتاب لأنه بالالتزام وهو بالشرط ومن شرطه ان لا يكون المولى من العرب لان تناصرهم بالقبائل فاغنى عن الموالاة. قال وللمولى ان ينتقل عنه بولائه الى غيره مالم يعقل عنه لانه عقد غير لازم بمنزلة الوصية وكذا للاعلي ان يتبرأ عن ولائه لعدم اللزوم الا انه يشترط فى هذا ان يكون بمحضر من الآخر كما في عزل الوكيل قصدًا بخلاف ما اذا عقد الاسفل مع غيره بغير محضر من الأول لانه فسخ حكمى بمنزلة العزل الحكمي في الوكالة. قال واذا عقل عنه لم يكن له ان يتحول بولائه الى غيره لانه تعلق به حق الغير ولانه قضى به القاضى ولانه بمنزلة عوض ناله كالعوض فى الهبة وكذا لا يتحول ولده وكذا اذا عقل عن ولده لم يكن لكل واحد منهما ان يتحول لانهما فى حق الولاء كشخص واحد . قال وليس لمولى العتاقة ان يوالى احدا لانه لازم ومع بقائه لا يظهر الادنى ... تشریح قوله وان كان وارث ..... الخ - اگر اس نو مسلم موالات کنندہ کا کوئی وارث ہو تو وہ اس کے مولیٰ سے مقدم ہوگا یعنی میراث اسی کو ملے گی مولی کو نہیں ملے گی ۔ اگر چہ وہ وارث اس کی پھوپھی یا خالہ یا ذ والا رحام میں سے کوئی ہو ۔ اس لئے کہ عقد فان قلت لم قيد بقوله قصداً قلت لأن عزل الوكيل بدون علمه يجوز فكذا عقد الولاء ينفسخ نسباً لا قصداً ٢ ١
He said: If he has an heir, he is more entitled to it [the inheritance] than him, even if that heir is a paternal aunt, maternal aunt, or others from among the dhawi al-arham. Because the contract of muwalat is between the two of them [the contracting parties], so it is not binding upon others. And a dhu rahm is an heir. And the conditions of inheritance and aqila are necessary, as mentioned in the book, because it [the contract] is by obligation and it is by condition. And among its conditions is that the mawla is not from the Arabs, because their mutual support is through tribes, thus they are independent of muwalat. He said: It is permissible for the mawla [the lower one] to transfer his wala to another as long as he has not paid [anything] on his behalf, because the contract is not binding, similar to a bequest. Likewise, it is permissible for the higher mawla to disclaim his wala due to non-bindingness, except that it is conditional that this be in the presence of the other, just as in the intentional removal of an agent. Contrary to when the lower mawla makes a contract with another without the presence of the first, because it is a constructive annulment, like the constructive removal in agency. He said: If he pays on his behalf, then he cannot transfer his wala to another, because the right of another has become attached to it, and because the judge has ruled it, and because it is like a compensation he obtained, like compensation in a gift. Likewise, his child cannot transfer. Likewise, if he pays on behalf of his child, neither of them may transfer, because they are like one person with respect to the right of wala. He said: The mawla of emancipation [wala al-itaqa] does not have the right to contract muwalat with anyone, because it is binding and as long as it remains, the lesser does not appear. Commentary: His statement: 'And if he has an heir...' Explanation: If this new Muslim who contracted muwalat has any heir, that heir takes precedence over his mawla, meaning the inheritance goes to him, not to the mawla, even if the heir is his paternal aunt, maternal aunt, or any dhu rahm. Because the contract... If it is said: why did he restrict with 'intentionally'? I say: because the removal of an agent without his knowledge is permissible, so likewise the contract of wala is annulled constructively, not intentionally.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings of the contract of wala al-muwalat. First, if the contract-maker (the convert) has any heir, even a dhu rahm (uterine relative), that heir takes precedence over the mawla. The contract only binds the two parties, so others cannot be excluded from inheritance. Second, the contract requires conditions: (1) the parties agree to mutual inheritance and aqila (mutual liability for blood money), and (2) the mawla must not be an Arab, because Arabs already have tribal support and do not need such contracts. Third, before any payment is made, the contract is non-binding; the mawla may transfer his wala to another, and the superior mawla (the patron) may disclaim it, but in the presence of the other, similar to intentional removal of an agent. If the mawla pays anything (e.g., blood money), the contract becomes binding, and neither he nor his child can transfer. Similarly, if he pays on behalf of his child, both are bound. Fourth, a mawla who already has wala al-itaqa (from emancipation) cannot contract muwalat because his existing wala is binding and leaves no room for a lesser form.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): In this context, the person who contracts muwalat with the convert. Also used for the emancipator in wala al-itaqa.
عاقلہ (aqila): Mutual liability for blood money; a condition in wala al-muwalat that the mawla pays diyah on behalf of the convert.
فسخ حكمى (faskh hukmi): Constructive annulment of a contract by action, without explicit words, as when the lower mawla contracts with another without the first party's presence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the contract of wala al-muwalat become binding and non-transferable?
A) Immediately upon signing the contract.
B) Only after the mawla pays blood money or similar compensation on behalf of the convert.
C) After one year has passed.
D) It never becomes binding; it can always be revoked.

Key Takeaway
Wala al-muwalat is a valid but initially revocable contract that becomes binding once the mawla fulfills his financial obligations, ensuring the convert's patronage and inheritance rights.

Transfer of Wala' al-Muwalat: When It Becomes Irrevocable
Explains when muwalat becomes binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which wala' al-muwalat becomes irrevocable and explain why it is initially non-binding.

Core Idea
Muwalat is like a will: freely changeable until the patron pays blood money — then it binds permanently.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies a key aspect of wala' al-muwalat. Unlike other forms of clientage, muwalat is initially revocable by either party. However, once the patron actually fulfills the obligation of paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of the client, the bond becomes irrevocable. Understanding this timing is essential for correctly applying the rules of muwalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the client's transfer of muwalat to another patron. The default ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) because the contract is non-binding (ghayr lazim).
This is analogous to a will, which can be changed before death.
2
Identify the condition that changes the ruling
The condition is whether the patron has paid any blood money (diyah) as 'aqila on behalf of the client. If no payment has occurred, transfer is allowed; if payment has occurred, transfer is prohibited.
Payment creates a right that attaches to the patron.
3
Understand the rationale
Before payment, muwalat is like a will — non-binding and revocable. After payment, it becomes like a gift with consideration (hiba bi'l-iwad), which is binding once the consideration is received.
The judge's declaration of the patron as 'aqila also supports bindingness.
4
Apply the analogy to agency revocation
The text compares the client transferring without the patron's knowledge to a principal selling property himself after appointing an agent; the agent is judicially removed. This shows the transfer is valid even without the first patron's knowledge.
This emphasizes the non-binding nature before payment.
5
Determine the scope of binding effect
Once the patron pays diyah, the clientage becomes binding not only on the client but also on his descendants. Similarly, if the patron pays on behalf of the client's son, all parties are treated as one entity regarding the right of clientage.
This permanent bond prevents any future transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وللمولى ان ينتقل .... الخ نومسلم موالات کنندہ کو جائز ہے کہ جس سے اس نے موالات کی ہے اس کی موالات سے پھر کر دوسرے سے موالات کرلے بشرطیکہ مولائے اول نے اس کی طرف سے عاقلہ ہو کر کچھ ادا نہ کیا ہو اس لئے کہ یہ عقد بمنزلہ وصیت کے لازمی نہیں ہے۔ اسی عدم لزوم کی وجہ سے مولائے اعلیٰ بھی اس کی ولاء کو چھوڑ سکتا ہے بشرطیکہ یہ ترک دوسرے کی موجودگی میں ہو۔ جیسے بالقصد وکیل کی معزولی میں ہوتا ہے۔ ہاں اگر نو مسلم نے مولائے اول کے علم کے بغیر دوسرے سے موالات کرتی تو یہ جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ حکمی فتح ہے۔ جیسے اگر بیع کے لئے کسی کو وکیل کیا پھر خود فروخت کر دیا تو وکیل حکما معزول ہو جاتا ہے۔
قوله واذا عقل عنه .... الخ - اگر مولائے اول نے اس کی طرف سے عاقلہ ہو کر جرمانہ ادا کیا ہو تو پھر اس کی ولاء سے منتقل نہیں ہوسکتا۔ کیونکہ اس کے ساتھ غیر کا حق وابستہ ہو گیا ہے۔ نیز قاضی اس کے مولیٰ کو عاقلہ قرار دے کر اس پر دیمت کا حکم کر چکا ۔ اور اس لئے بھی کہ یہ بمنزلہ ایک عوض کے ہے جو اس نے حاصل کر لیا ۔ جیسے ہبہ کا عوض لینے کے بعد رجوع نہیں کر سکتا بلکہ آئندہ اس کی اولاد بھی اس ولا ء سے نہیں پھر سکتی۔ اسی طرح اگر مولیٰ نے اس کے فرزند کی طرف سے عاقلہ ہو کر مال دیا تو ان میں سے کوئی بھی ولاء سے نہیں پھر سکتا کیونکہ حق ولاء میں مولی اعلیٰ مولی اسفل اور اس کا فرزند سب ہمنزلہ ایک شخص کے ہیں۔
His statement: 'And the client has the right to transfer...' - It is permissible for the new Muslim who has made muwalat to leave the muwalat of the one with whom he made it and make muwalat with another, on condition that the first patron has not paid anything as 'aqila on his behalf. Because this contract is like a will; it is not binding. Due to this lack of binding, the superior patron can also abandon his clientage, provided that this abandonment occurs in the presence of another. As happens intentionally in the removal of an agent. Yes, if the new Muslim makes muwalat with another without the knowledge of the first patron, it is permissible, because this is a judicial conquest. Like if someone appointed an agent for a sale and then sold it himself, the agent is judicially removed. His statement: 'And when he has paid blood money for him...' - If the first patron has paid a penalty as 'aqila on his behalf, then he cannot transfer from his clientage. Because the right of another has become attached to him. Also, the judge has declared the patron as 'aqila and passed a judgment of liability against him. And also because this is like a consideration (iwad) that he has obtained. Like a gift, after taking consideration one cannot revoke. Rather, in the future his descendants also cannot turn away from this clientage. Similarly, if the patron has given money as 'aqila on behalf of his child, then none of them can turn away from the clientage, because in the right of clientage, the superior patron, the inferior client, and his child are all in the position of one person.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the transferability of wala' al-muwalat. The author explains that before the patron has paid any blood money (diyah) on behalf of the client, the client is free to transfer his clientage to another person. This is because the contract is not binding; it is analogous to a will (wasiyyah). Similarly, the patron may also abandon the clientage by declaring so in the presence of another person, analogous to the removal of an agent by the principal. If the client transfers without the first patron's knowledge, it is valid and considered a 'judicial conquest' (fat'h hukmi) – similar to when a principal sells the property himself after appointing an agent, which automatically cancels the agency. However, once the patron has actually paid any portion of the blood money as 'aqila, the clientage becomes binding and cannot be transferred. The reason is that a third party's right (the victim's right to diyah) has been satisfied, and the patron has provided a consideration ('iwad). Just as a gift cannot be revoked after the recipient gives a consideration, the clientage becomes irrevocable. Moreover, this binding effect extends to the client's descendants; they also cannot leave this clientage. Similarly, if the patron has paid blood money on behalf of the client's son, then all parties – patron, client, and client's son – are considered as one entity in terms of the right of clientage, and none can separate. This is a strong indication of the Hanafi position that muwalat, once supported by actual payment, becomes a permanent bond.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqila): The group responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a killer; in muwalat, the patron assumes this role.
لزوم (luzum): Binding nature of a contract; muwalat is initially non-binding (ghayr lazim) but becomes binding after the patron pays diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does wala' al-muwalat become irrevocable (lazim) such that the client cannot transfer?
A) Immediately upon contracting.
B) After the client has accepted Islam.
C) After the patron has paid blood money (diyah) on behalf of the client.
D) After the patron has declared acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Muwalat is non-binding until the patron pays blood money; payment makes it irrevocable for the client and his descendants.

Conditions and Transfer of Wala' in the Mawalat Contract
Explains conditions of mawalat and permissibility of transferring wala'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of a valid mawalat contract and apply the Hanafi ruling on when a client may transfer his wala' to another patron.

Core Idea
A mawalat contract is non-binding like a will until the patron pays bloodwit — then it becomes binding and inalienable.

The contract of mawalat (clientage) is a unique legal institution in Hanafi fiqh that creates inheritance and bloodwit obligations between a non-Arab Muslim and his patron. This lesson explains the essential conditions for its validity and then examines the key ruling: the client may freely transfer his wala' (patronage) to another patron only so long as the first patron has not yet paid any bloodwit on his behalf. You will see how the Hanafi school grounds this non-binding nature in a qiyas (analogy) to wills and further supports it with analogies to agency and gift law.

Guided Path
1
List the conditions of mawalat
Identify the three conditions stated in the text: (1) the client must not be an Arab, (2) the contract must explicitly mention inheritance and bloodwit, and (3) both parties must be sane, adult, and free.
Mawalat of a minor or slave without permission is invalid.
2
State the ruling on transfer of wala'
The text states that the client may transfer his wala' to another patron provided the first patron has not paid any bloodwit on his behalf. This is the permissibility ruling.
The patron may also abandon his wala' in favor of another, but only in the presence of another.
3
Understand the qiyas (analogy) to a will
The author likens mawalat to a will (wasiyya) because both are gratuitous and revocable prior to performance. Just as a will is non-binding until the testator's death, mawalat is non-binding until the patron actually pays bloodwit.
This is the central proof for the Hanafi position.
4
Apply the analogies to agency and gift
The text uses two further analogies: (a) if the client switches patrons without the first patron's knowledge, it is valid — like an agent dismissed when the principal acts directly; (b) after payment, the wala' becomes binding — like a gift after compensation, which cannot be revoked.
These analogies reinforce the non-binding/binding boundary.
5
Identify the binding point and its effect
Once the first patron has paid bloodwit (acted as 'aqila), the wala' becomes binding. The client cannot transfer, and the binding effect extends to the patron's children.
The judge's ruling also confirms the patron as 'aqila, creating a third-party right.

3. He should not be from the Arabs. 4. In the contract of mawalat, inheritance and bloodwit should be mentioned. 5. The two contracting parties should be sane, adult, and free. Because the mawalat of a minor without the permission of a guardian, and the mawalat of a slave without the permission of a master, is invalid. Book of Wala'. His saying: 'And it is permissible for the patron to transfer...' etc. - It is permissible for a new Muslim who made mawalat to leave the mawalat of the one with whom he made it and make mawalat with another, provided that the first patron has not paid anything as 'aqila on his behalf. Because this contract is like a will, not binding. Due to this non-bindingness, the superior patron may also abandon his wala', provided that this abandonment occurs in the presence of another. Just as occurs in the deliberate dismissal of an agent. Yes, if the new Muslim makes mawalat with another without the knowledge of the first patron, then it is permissible. Because this is a judicial opening. Like if someone appointed an agent for a sale and then sold it himself, the agent becomes judicially dismissed. His saying: 'And when he pays bloodwit on his behalf...' etc. - If the first patron paid bloodwit as 'aqila on his behalf, then he cannot transfer from his wala'. Because a right of another has become attached to it. Also, the judge has already ruled the patron as his 'aqila and imposed the blood money on him. And also because this is like a compensation that he has obtained. Just as after taking compensation for a gift, he cannot revoke. Rather, his children in the future cannot turn away from this wala'. Similarly, if the patron paid money on behalf of his son as 'aqila, then none of them can turn away from the wala', because in the right of wala', the superior patron, the inferior patron, and his son are all like one person.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, a commentary on al-Hidaya, discusses the contract of mawalat (clientage) and the transfer of wala' (right of patronage). The passage begins by listing conditions for the validity of mawalat: the client must not be an Arab (because Arabs inherit by asabah, not need clientage), the contract must explicitly mention inheritance and bloodwit (the two core obligations of clientage), and both parties must be sane, adult, and free. Mawalat by a minor without guardian's permission or by a slave without master's permission is invalid. The commentary then explains the ruling that it is permissible for the new Muslim (the client) to transfer his wala' from one patron to another, as long as the first patron has not yet paid any bloodwit ('aqila) on his behalf. This is because the mawalat contract is non-binding, analogous to a will (wasiyya). The patron can also abandon his wala' in favor of another patron, similar to the deliberate dismissal of an agent (wakil). If the client switches patrons without the first patron's knowledge, it is also valid, as the first patron is considered judicially dismissed like an agent who is dismissed when the principal sells directly. However, once the first patron has paid bloodwit on behalf of the client, the wala' becomes binding and cannot be transferred. This is because a third-party right (the recipient of the bloodwit) has been attached, and the judge has confirmed the patron as the 'aqila. It is analogous to a gift (hiba) where the donor cannot revoke after receiving compensation. The binding effect extends to the patron's children, who also cannot later renounce the wala', since all parties in the chain of wala' are considered as one entity in terms of rights.

Key Terms
موالات (muwālāt): A contract of clientage between a non-Arab Muslim and a Muslim patron, where the patron inherits from the client and pays bloodwit on his behalf.
ولاء (walā'): The right of patronage that includes inheritance from the client and responsibility for bloodwit.
عاقلہ ('āqila): The group or person responsible for paying bloodwit (diya) on behalf of the client; in mawalat, it is the patron.
عقد موالات ('aqd al-muwālāt): The contract of clientage that creates a relationship of mutual inheritance and bloodwit responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon signing the contract
B) When the patron pays bloodwit ('aqila) on behalf of the client
C) When the client dies
D) When the judge declares it binding

Key Takeaway
The mawalat contract is non-binding like a will until the patron actually pays bloodwit; after payment it becomes binding and inalienable.

Definition and Classification of Ikrah (Coercion) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and classification of coercion with legal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ikrah, identify its two types (complete and incomplete), and apply the ruling to determine legal effects on contracts and prohibited acts.

Core Idea
Coercion negates consent or corrupts choice; complete coercion nullifies all transactions, incomplete only those requiring consent.

Coercion (ikrah) is a legal circumstance that can change the ruling of an act from prohibition to permissibility, but its effect depends on the severity of the threat. In this lesson, we unpack the Hanafi definition of coercion, the condition for its establishment, and the critical distinction between complete and incomplete coercion. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining whether a coerced contract is void or merely voidable, and which prohibited acts become permissible under duress.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the legal definition of ikrah
Read the Arabic definition: ikrah is an action that negates consent or corrupts choice while legal capacity remains. Note that both elements are disjunctive: either loss of consent or corruption of choice is sufficient.
The definition excludes acts that completely remove capacity (e.g., insanity).
2
2. Understand the condition for establishment
Ikrah is established only when the threat comes from a person able to execute it, whether a ruler or a thief. This ensures the threat is credible and the victim's fear is reasonable.
An empty threat or one from an incapable person does not constitute legal coercion.
3
3. Distinguish between complete and incomplete coercion
Complete coercion (ikrah mulji') involves a threat of death or loss of a limb; it corrupts both consent and choice. Incomplete coercion (ikrah ghayr mulji') involves lesser threats (e.g., imprisonment, beating); it negates consent but leaves choice intact.
In the source, 'loss of consent is more general than corruption of choice'.
4
4. Apply the classification to legal effects
Complete coercion invalidates all transactions (sale, lease, etc.) because choice is corrupted. Incomplete coercion invalidates only those acts that require consent for validity (like sale, acknowledgment). Prohibited acts like eating carrion become permissible under both types, but murder remains haram.
Uttering disbelief is a concession (rukhsah) under coercion, but the coerced is still accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The book of coercion. Coercion establishes its ruling when it occurs from one who is able to execute what he threatens, whether a sultan or a thief, because coercion is a name for an action that a person does to another, whereby his consent is negated or his choice is corrupted while his legal capacity remains. Translation: The ruling of coercion is established when it occurs from one who is able to execute what he threatens, whether a sultan or a thief. Because coercion is the name of an action that a person carries out upon another, by which his consent is removed or his choice becomes corrupt while his capacity remains. Elaboration: His saying 'The book of...' - Through the contract of clientage (mawalat), the status of the higher patron changes from prohibition to permissibility. Meaning: if there were no clientage, taking the wealth of the lower patron would not be permissible. When the contract of clientage occurs, now taking his wealth becomes permissible. Similarly, in coercion, the addressed person's status changes from prohibition to permissibility: that is, that which was prohibited before coercion becomes permissible after coercion. Therefore, after the book of clientage, they placed the book of coercion. His saying 'Coercion...etc.' - Linguistically, coercion means compelling someone and inciting them to an undesirable matter. In the terminology of jurists, coercion is an action that a person does to another in such a way that his consent is removed and his choice becomes corrupted. Or, coercion has two types: complete (muli') and incomplete (ghayr muli'). Complete coercion is that in which the coerced person fears loss of his life or a limb, such as threat of killing or threat of amputation, etc. In this case, the consent of the coerced is lost and choice is corrupted. Incomplete coercion is that in which there is no fear of loss of life or limb, but only consent is lost, such as in the case of threat of imprisonment or threat of beating, etc. So loss of consent is more general than corruption of choice because the opposite of consent is dislike (karahah), and the opposite of choice is compulsion (jabr). In coercion by imprisonment and beating, undoubtedly dislike exists, so consent is absent. But choice exists with the attribute of soundness. Because choice is corrupted only when there is fear of loss of life or a limb. Thus, incomplete coercion will only be effective in those transactions that require consent, such as sale, acknowledgment, lease, etc. Complete coercion will be effective in all transactions. Benefit: Coercion, whether complete or incomplete, neither invalidates the capacity of the coerced nor does it remove the demand (of the law). The proof for this is that the actions of the coerced fluctuate between obligation and license, prohibition and permission. Also, sometimes he becomes sinful and sometimes deserving of reward. For example, killing a person, highway robbery, and adultery are prohibited for him; so if he does them, he is sinful, and if he refrains, he will receive reward. Similarly, eating carrion and drinking wine are permissible for him, and uttering words of disbelief is a license. Shaykh Fakhr al-Islam.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the chapter on coercion (ikrah) in al-Hidayah. It first states that coercion takes legal effect only when it comes from someone who has the power to carry out the threat, whether that person is a ruler or a thief. The reason is that coercion is defined as an action that negates the victim's consent or corrupts his choice, while his legal capacity (ahliyyah) remains intact. The Urdu commentary elaborates that the ruling of coercion is established under these conditions. The author then explains why the book of coercion follows the book of clientage (mawalat): both cause a change from prohibition to permissibility. In clientage, the higher patron's taking of the lower patron's wealth becomes permissible; in coercion, an otherwise prohibited act becomes permissible due to duress. Next, the text distinguishes between complete coercion (ikrah mulji') and incomplete coercion (ikrah ghayr mulji'). Complete coercion involves a threat of death or loss of a limb, which destroys both consent and valid choice. Incomplete coercion involves a lesser threat (e.g., imprisonment or beating), which destroys consent but leaves choice intact. The legal effect: incomplete coercion invalidates acts that require consent (like sale, acknowledgment, lease), while complete coercion invalidates all transactions. Importantly, in both types, the coerced person's legal capacity remains, and he is still held accountable for some acts (e.g., murder remains haram, but he may be sinful if he commits it, and rewarded if he refrains). Certain prohibitions like eating carrion or drinking wine become permissible under duress, and uttering words of disbelief is a concession (rukhsah).

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): An action that negates consent or corrupts choice while capacity remains; established when threat is from someone able to execute it.
رضا (rida): Consent; its negation is a key element of ikrah. Absent in both types of coercion.
اختیار (ikhtiyār): Choice; corrupted only in complete coercion (fear of death/limb), remains in incomplete coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Coercion negates consent or corrupts choice; complete coercion voids all transactions, incomplete only those requiring consent, and in both cases legal capacity remains.

Definition of Ikrah and Its Conditions
Defines ikrah and explains conditions for its establishment, including the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ikrah, state its two conditions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute about who qualifies as a coercer.

Core Idea
Ikrah requires a capable coercer and credible fear; fatwa follows the broader view of the Sahibayn.

Coercion (ikrah) voids or corrupts consent, affecting contracts and oaths. But not every threat counts. Hanafi law sets two conditions to distinguish genuine coercion from mere pressure. The first condition sparked a famous difference between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples—a difference later resolved as one of context, not principle.

Guided Path
1
Define the legal concept of ikrah
Identify the core definition from the text: ikrah is an act that negates consent or corrupts choice while legal capacity remains. Distinguish between complete and incomplete coercion.
Complete coercion involves threat to life or limb; incomplete involves lesser harm like beating or imprisonment.
2
Extract the first condition – capability of the coercer
The text states that the coercer must have the actual ability to carry out the threat. Note the dispute between Abu Hanifa (only sultan) and the Sahibayn (any capable person).
Ability here means power (qudra) plus enforcement capacity (man'a).
3
Analyze the dispute and its resolution
Understand the reasoning: Abu Hanifa argued that only the sultan has the power to enforce threats; the Sahibayn said ability alone suffices. Later scholars framed this as a difference of era, not evidence. The fatwa adopts the Sahibayn's view.
The practical consequence: today, a thief with a weapon can be a valid coercer.
4
Extract the second condition – credible fear
The coerced must have a dominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the threat will be executed. Mere fear or suspicion is insufficient.
This ensures the coercion is real, not imagined. The text links it to the coercer's capability.
5
Apply the definition to a hypothetical
Test: A masked robber threatens to kill a shopkeeper unless he signs a sale. The robber has a gun (capability) and the shopkeeper genuinely believes he will shoot (dominant presumption). This is ikrah. If the threat is from an unarmed child, it is not ikrah.
Sahibayn's view allows the robber to be a coercer; Abu Hanifa would require the robber to be the sultan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاكراه يثبت حكمه اذا حصل ممن يقدر على ايقاع ما يوعد به سلطانا كان او لصا لان الاكراه اسم لفعل يفعله المرء بغيره فینتفی به رضاه او یفسد به اختیاره مع بقاء اهليته وهذا انما يتحقق اذا خاف المكره تحقيق ما يوعد به وذلك انما يكون من القادر والسلطان وغيره سيان عند تحقق القدرة والذى قاله ابو حنيفة ان الاكراه لا يتحقق الا من السلطان لما أن المنعة له والقدرة لا يتحقق بدون المنعة فقد قالوا هذا اختلاف عصر و زمان لا اختلاف حجة وبرهان ولم يكن القدرة في زمنه الا للسلطان ثم بعد ذلك تغير الزمان واهله ثم كما يشترط قدرة المكره لتحقق الاكراه يشترط حرف المكره وقوع ما هدد به و ذلك بان يغلب على ظنه انه يفعله ليصير به محمولا على ما دعى اليه من الفعل
Coercion establishes its ruling when it occurs from one who is able to bring about what he threatens, whether sultan or thief, because coercion is a name for an action that a person does to another, whereby his consent is negated or his choice is corrupted while his legal capacity remains. And this is only realized when the coerced person fears the realization of what he is threatened with, and that is only from one who is able, and the sultan and others are equal when ability is present. What Abu Hanifa said is that coercion is only realized from the sultan because he has power, and ability cannot be realized without power. They said this is a difference of time and era, not a difference of proof and evidence. Ability in his time was only with the sultan, then after that the time and its people changed. Then just as the ability of the coercer is a condition for the realization of coercion, it is also a condition that the coerced person fears the occurrence of what he is threatened with, such that he has a dominant presumption that he will do it, so that he becomes compelled thereby to the action to which he is called.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah). It first defines coercion: it is an act done by one person to another that negates the latter's consent or corrupts his choice, while his legal capacity remains intact. Two types are distinguished: complete coercion (ikrah mujbi) involving threat to life or limb, and incomplete coercion (ikrah ghayr mujbi) involving lesser threats like imprisonment or beating. Complete coercion nullifies both consent and choice, while incomplete only negates consent. For coercion to be legally effective, two conditions must be met. First, the coercer must be capable of carrying out the threat. The text records a difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: Abu Hanifa held that only a sultan (ruler) can genuinely coerce because he alone has the power (man'a) to enforce threats. The Sahibayn argued that anyone with actual ability, whether sultan or thief, can coerce. The later scholars reconciled this as a difference of time not principle: in Abu Hanifa's era, only the sultan had such power; later, others could. The relied-upon fatwa is that of the Sahibayn. Second, the coerced person must have a dominant presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the threat will be carried out; mere fear is insufficient if the threat is not credible. This ensures that the coercion is genuine and not merely imagined.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): An act done by one person to another that negates the latter's consent or corrupts his choice while his legal capacity remains.
سلطان (sultan): The ruler or authority who has power (man'a) to enforce threats.
ظن غالب (ghalabat al-zann): Dominant presumption; a high probability that the threat will be carried out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, who can be a valid coercer (mukrih)?
A) Only the sultan
B) Any person with actual ability to carry out the threat
C) Only a judge
D) Only a person appointed by the state

Key Takeaway
Ikrah legally exists when a capable coercer makes a credible threat that the victim believes will be carried out; the fatwa follows the broader view of the Sahibayn, not the restriction of Abu Hanifa.

Conditions for Valid Coercion and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Coercer's Power
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the coercer's authority and conditions for coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid coercion (ikrah) in Hanafi law and explain why the mufta bihi position on the coercer’s capability shifted from Abu Hanifa’s view to that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Coercion requires a capable coercer and the coerced’s prevailing fear; today, any powerful person suffices.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies when coercion (ikrah) is legally recognized. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa initially held that only the ruler (sultan) could coerce because he alone possessed full power (man'a), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argued that anyone capable of carrying out the threat qualifies. The later scholars (mashayikh) resolved this as a difference of time, not of proof, and the fatwa now follows the Sahibayn’s view. Understanding this development teaches us how Hanafi fiqh adapts to changing circumstances while preserving legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category and its conditions
Recognize that ikrah (coercion) is a legal category with specific conditions for validity. The passage explicitly states two conditions: (a) the coercer must be capable (qudra) of carrying out the threat, and (b) the coerced must have a prevailing belief (ghalabat al-zann) that the threat will be executed.
These conditions are consistent across the Hanafi school; the dispute is only about the first condition's scope.
2
Trace Imam Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa held that coercion is only realized from the sultan because only he possesses man'a (power/authority). In his era, no one else had the practical capability to enforce a threat.
This was a reality-based ijtihad, not a principle that the sultan has a monopoly on coercion.
3
Note the Sahibayn's position and its dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani held that any capable person—ruler, thief, bandit—can effect coercion. Their evidence: the threat itself eliminates free consent regardless of the threatener's official status.
They considered qudra (capability) sufficient; man'a is a component of qudra, not a separate condition.
4
Analyze the later tarjih and resolution
The mashayikh (later scholars) harmonized the positions by calling it an ikhtilaf-e-zaman (difference of era). In Abu Hanifa's time, only the sultan had man'a; later, power became diffused. Therefore, the Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
This shows how Hanafi fatwa may change when the effective cause (illa) of a ruling shifts context.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a modern scenario
Today, a thief who threatens to kill someone unless they sign a contract is considered a valid coercer. The contract signed under such duress is voidable due to lack of free consent.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): coercion claims are no longer limited to official authorities.

And this is realized when the coerced person fears the fulfillment of what he is threatened with. And that can only be from one who is capable, whether the ruler or another, they are equal when capability exists. What Abu Hanifa said is that coercion is not realized except from the ruler because he has power and capability is not realized without power. So they said: this is a difference of era and time, not a difference of proof and evidence. In his time, capability was only with the ruler; then after that, the time and its people changed. Then, just as the capability of the coercer is a condition for the realization of coercion, so too is the [fear] of the coerced that the threat will occur, such that his prevailing belief is that he will do it, so that he becomes compelled to do what he is urged to do. (2) The coerced must have a prevailing belief that the threat will be carried out.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding who qualifies as a coercer (mukrih). The text establishes two conditions for valid coercion: (a) the coercer must have the capability to carry out the threat (qudra), and (b) the coerced must have a prevailing belief (ghalabat al-zann) that the threat will be executed. Abu Hanifa initially held that only the ruler (sultan) has such capability because in his era, only the ruler possessed full authority (man'a). However, the later scholars (mashayikh) resolved this as a difference in time, not in evidence. Since eras changed and power is now available to others, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, that any capable person—whether ruler, thief, or other—can effect coercion. This is consistent with Hanafi methodology where fatwa adapts to changing circumstances when the underlying illa (effective cause) remains but its manifestation changes.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrih): The person who coerces by threatening harm
مكره (mukrah): The person who is coerced
قدرة (qudra): Capability to carry out the threatened harm
ظن غالب (ghalabat al-zann): Prevailing belief that the threat will be executed

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The coercer must be a non-Muslim and the coerced must be a Muslim.
D) The coercion must be by severe beatings only.

Key Takeaway
Valid coercion requires both the coercer's capability and the coerced's prevailing fear; the Hanafi fatwa now recognizes any capable person as a coercer, not only the sultan.

Contracts under Duress: Severe vs. Mild Threats
Ruling with proof on duress and threat levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between severe and mild threats in determining duress (ikrah) and apply the Hanafi ruling that contracts under severe duress are voidable (fasid) while those under mild threats are valid and binding.

Core Idea
Duress nullifies consent; severe threats make a contract voidable, mild threats do not.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on contracts entered under duress. The passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains that the validity of a contract hinges on the presence of mutual consent. When a person is compelled by a severe threat—such as death or severe beating—the resulting contract is voidable (fasid) at the option of the coerced party. However, mild threats, like a single whip or one day's imprisonment, do not constitute duress unless the person is of high status. We will also see how the Quranic verse on mutual consent is used as proof and how the Hanafi school distinguishes between types of threats.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling under duress
The act is a contract (sale, lease) entered under threat. The ruling: the contract is concluded but voidable; the coerced person has the option to annul after duress ends.
2
Locate the Quranic dalil
The proof is Surah An-Nisa 4:29: 'except that it be a trade by mutual consent.' This establishes that mutual consent is a condition for valid trade.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase '‘an taradin' (by mutual consent) indicates that consent is an essential condition. The absence of consent due to duress makes the contract voidable, not automatically void.
4
Distinguish severe threats from mild threats
Severe threats (death, severe beating, long imprisonment) constitute duress. Mild threats (one whip, one day imprisonment) do not, unless the person is of high status (e.g., a judge) for whom such threats are disgraceful.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For irrevocable acts (divorce, manumission), the three Imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) hold that duress prevents the act from taking effect, while Abu Hanifa holds they become binding. For sale, they agree on voidability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

میں اکراہ سے مراد اس کے لغوی معنی ہیں۔ یعنی حمل الانسان علی امریکرہہ اور یہ معنی صور مذکور میں بلا شک وشبہ متحقق ہیں اور فلای تحقق بہ الاکراہ سے جس اکراہ کی نفی کی ہے وہ شرعی معنی کے لحاظ سے ہے جس پر اس کے احکام مرتب ہوتے ہیں۔ فلا تناقض یہ بھی کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ شروع میں اکراہ سے تعبیر کرنا بطریق مشاکلت ہے۔ جیسے قول باری تعالیٰ میں ہے۔ تعلم ما في نفسي ولا اعلم ما في نفسک اس صورت میں لفظ اکراہ حقیقت نہ ہو گا نہ لغویہ اور نہ شرعیہ، بلکہ مجاز ہو جائے گا۔ فلا تناقض - قوله الا اذا كان الرجل .... الخ ۔ ہاں اگر وہ مگر شخص جس کو ضرب سوط یا حبس یوم کی دھمکی دی گئی ہے قاضی وغیرہ کے مانند وجیہ ومعزز ہو جس کے حق میں ایک روز کی قید یا ایک کوڑا بھی بے عزتی ہے۔ حتی کہ مجلس و مجمع میں اس کی گوشمالی بھی باعث اہانت ہے تو اس کے حق میں اتنی معمولی تہدید بھی اکراہ ہے۔ بعض شوافع ، امام مالک اور ایک روایت میں امام احمد بھی اسی کے قائل ہیں اور دوسری روایت یہ ہے کہ یہ وعیدا کراہ نہیں ہے۔ قاضی شریح سے منقول ہے کہ قید و وعید اور ضرب وشتم لوگوں کے اختلافات حالات کے اعتبار سے اکراہ ہے۔
When a man is compelled to sell, buy, acknowledgement, or lease his house under threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment, and he sells or buys, he has the option after the duress ends to either enforce or annul the contract. This is because, in our school, all transactions of a coerced person are concluded in terms of speech. Contracts that can be annulled, like sale and lease, he can annul; those that cannot be annulled, like divorce, marriage, manumission, tadbir, istila', and vows, become binding, except according to the three Imams. The reason is that duress eliminates consent, while mutual consent is a condition for the validity of these contracts, as Allah says: 'except that it be a trade by mutual consent.' So with the mentioned threats, consent is lost, making the contract voidable because the absence of a condition necessitates the absence of the conditioned. A question: although the verse implies prohibition when consent is absent, the verse 'Allah permitted sale and prohibited usury' generally allows sale even without consent. Answer: linguistically, sale is exchange of property by mutual consent; what the Shari'ah agrees with actual meaning is the principle. Another question: the beginning of the verse 'do not consume your property among yourselves wrongfully' indicates that sale is void. Answer: 'wrongfully' means what is not permitted by Shari'ah, like theft, betrayal, usurpation, gambling, and usurious contracts, and 'by mutual consent' is an attribute of trade, i.e., trade proceeding from consent. The text says: 'contrary to if he is threatened with one whip, one day's imprisonment, or one day in fetters' - such minor threats do not constitute duress because customarily they are not heeded. A question: the author's last statement contradicts his first, because 'contrary to if he is threatened' confirms duress in these cases, while 'it does not constitute duress' explicitly denies it. Answer: in 'contrary to if he is threatened', 'ikrah' is used in its literal sense: compelling someone to something disliked, which is undoubtedly present; while the denial of 'ikrah' refers to the Shari'ah meaning upon which rulings are based, so no contradiction. Alternatively, it is a metaphorical usage. The text then says: 'except if the person is a person of high status like a judge, such that one day's imprisonment or one whip is disgraceful, even public reproach is humiliating, then such minor threats are duress for him.' Some Shafi'is, Imam Malik, and in one narration Ahmad also hold this view; another narration says it is not duress. Qadi Shurayh is reported to say that imprisonment, threats, beating, and abuse are duress depending on people's differing conditions.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is an expansion of the Hidayah's ruling on contracts under duress. It begins by stating the core rule: if a person is compelled by threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment to sell, buy, or make an acknowledgement, after the duress ends he has the option to either uphold the contract or annul it. The reason given is that mutual consent is a condition for the validity of such contracts, and the verse (4:29) explicitly requires mutual consent. The passage then addresses potential objections: first, that the verse permitting trade (2:275) might allow sales even without consent—but the response clarifies that the very definition of sale (bay') linguistically includes mutual consent, so the Shari'ah follows real meaning. Second, that the verse prohibiting wrongful consumption (4:29) might void all sales without consent—but 'wrongful' refers specifically to prohibited means like theft or interest, not to the absence of consent. Next, the passage distinguishes between severe threats (death, severe beating, long imprisonment) which constitute duress, and mild threats (one whip, one day imprisonment) which customarily are not feared, and thus do not constitute duress. A seeming contradiction in the text is resolved by noting that 'ikrah' is used sometimes in its literal sense (compulsion to something disliked) and sometimes in its technical Shari'ah sense (which nullifies consent). An exception is made for persons of high status (e.g., a judge) for whom even a mild threat is disgraceful and thus constitutes duress. The passage notes that some Shafi'is and Malik, and in one narration Ahmad, agree with this exception, while another narration from Ahmad disagrees. Qadi Shurayh is quoted as saying that the determination of duress depends on people's differing conditions.

Key Terms
اكراه (ikrah): Compelling someone to do an act they dislike, under threat that induces fear; in Shari'ah, it nullifies consent.
رضاء (rida'): Consent given willingly without coercion; a condition for validity of contracts.
عقد فاسد (aqd fasid): A contract that is valid in essence but defective in a condition; it does not transfer ownership until delivery, and is voidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a normal person is threatened with a single whip to sell his house and he sells, what is the ruling on the sale?
A) The sale is void ab initio (batil).
B) The sale is valid and binding.
C) The sale is voidable (fasid); the seller has the option to annul.
D) The sale is suspended pending removal of duress.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi fiqh, severe duress (ikrah) makes a contract voidable (fasid), while mild threats do not affect validity unless the person is of high status.
