Ruling on Acknowledgement under Duress
Explains ruling on acknowledgement under duress with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgements made under duress and explain the legal reasoning (talil) that renders them invalid.

Core Idea
An acknowledgement under duress is invalid because the possibility of falsehood outweighs the presumption of truthfulness.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial nuance in the law of acknowledgements (iqrar). While an acknowledgement made voluntarily is binding because a person is presumed to be truthful against their own interest, duress disrupts this presumption. The Hidayah, through its commentaries, clarifies that acknowledgements made under threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment are invalid due to the probability of falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that the act is an acknowledgement (iqrar) made under duress. The ruling given in the passage is that it is invalid (fasid).
The ruling is not sahih (valid) or batil (void), but fasid (invalid) - meaning it is not binding but may have some legal effect if not repudiated?
2
Understand the legal basis (sabab)
The text explains that duress eliminates consent (rida) and raises the possibility that the acknowledgement is false (kidhb) – the person may have lied to avoid harm.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on probability, not a textual proof.
3
Contrast with voluntary acknowledgement
In voluntary acknowledgements, the context indicates truthfulness because people do not normally lie against their own interest. Under duress, that presumption is lost.
4
Note the scope of duress
The passage specifies severe duress: threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment. Not all forms of duress invalidate.
Minor threats may not rise to this level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وكذا الاقرار الخ - نہایہ ، عنایہ، بنایہ میں ہے کہ یہ قول سابق و الاكراه بهذه الاشياء يعدم الرضاء فيفسد پر معطوف ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ قتل و ضرب شدید اور حبس مدید کی تہدید سے اقرار بھی فاسد ہو جائے گا۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ اقرار فی نفسہ صدق و کذب کے درمیان دائر ہے۔ لیکن رضاء و خوشدلی کی صورت میں بدلالت حال کی جانب صدق راج ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ آدمی اپنے جی کی تکذیب نہیں کرتا اور اکراہ کی حالت میں یہ احتمال ہے کہ شاید اس نے دفع ضرر کی خاطر جھوٹا اقرار کیا ہو ۔ صاحب نتائج کی رائے یہ ہے کہ قول مذکور لان من شرط صحة سے فیفسد تک پورے مجموعہ پر معطوف ہے۔
The statement 'And likewise acknowledgement...' - in Nihayah, Inayah, and Binayah it is said that this is connected to the previous statement 'and duress with these things eliminates consent and thus makes it invalid.' The meaning is that acknowledgement under threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment is also invalid. The reason is that acknowledgement in itself hovers between truth and falsehood. But when there is consent and willingness, the context indicates that truthfulness is dominant because normally a person does not lie against himself. And under duress, there is a possibility that he made a false acknowledgement to avoid harm. The author of Nata'ij is of the opinion that the statement is connected to the entire passage from 'because from the conditions of validity' to 'makes it invalid'.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on acknowledgements (iqrar) made under duress. It first notes that according to the major commentaries (Nihayah, Inayah, Binayah), this point is connected to the earlier ruling that duress eliminates consent and makes contracts invalid. The same applies to acknowledgements: if a person acknowledges a debt or claim under threat of death, severe beating, or long imprisonment, the acknowledgement is invalid (fasid). The reason given is that an acknowledgement can be either true or false. When someone makes an acknowledgement willingly, the natural presumption is that it is true, because people do not ordinarily lie to their own detriment. However, under duress, there is a real possibility that the person is lying just to avoid harm. Therefore, the acknowledgement lacks credibility and is not binding. The commentator also notes that some scholars (like the author of Nata'ij) consider this statement to be connected to the entire preceding passage from 'because from the conditions of validity' up to 'makes it invalid', meaning it shares the same legal basis.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): A statement by a person acknowledging a right or obligation against himself, normally binding if truthful and voluntary.
صدق و کذب (sidq wa kadhib): Truth and falsehood; acknowledgement is between these two possibilities, but duress tips the balance toward falsehood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the person had no intention to speak.
B) Because the threat nullifies the contract of acknowledgement.
C) Because the possibility of falsehood outweighs the presumption of truthfulness under duress.
D) Because the acknowledgement was not made in writing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that acknowledgements made under severe duress are invalid because duress shifts the balance from presumed truth to probable falsehood.

Resolving the Apparent Contradiction in Coercion: Literal vs Legal Meaning
Resolves textual contradiction by distinguishing literal and legal meanings of ikrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the literal (lughawi) and legal (shar'i) meanings of ikrah and apply this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in Hanafi texts.

Core Idea
Coercion has two meanings: any compulsion (literal) versus compulsion that triggers legal consequences (legal).

In studying Hanafi fiqh, you may encounter texts that seem to contradict each other on whether a certain action constitutes coercion (ikrah). This passage from the Hidayah commentary provides a key methodological tool: distinguishing between the literal meaning of a term and its legal technical meaning. By understanding this, you can reconcile apparent contradictions and correctly apply the rulings of coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The passage mentions two statements: one suggesting a whip strike is coercion, another denying coercion by it. Understand that these appear contradictory.
The first statement: 'contrary to the case where he struck with a whip' implies a whip strike is coercion. The second: 'coercion is not realized by it' denies it.
2
Recognize the two senses of ikrah
The literal meaning is any compulsion to do something disliked. The legal meaning is compulsion that has specific legal consequences (e.g., invalidating contracts).
Hanafis distinguish these two senses; the legal definition requires specific conditions like severity and immediacy.
3
Apply the distinction
The first statement uses the literal meaning (a whip strike is compulsion), the second uses the legal meaning (it does not meet the threshold for legal consequences). Thus no contradiction.
This resolves the tension without rejecting either statement.
4
Consider the alternative explanation: mushaakalah
The word 'coercion' may be used metaphorically for stylistic congruence, similar to Quranic usage (e.g., 'You know what is in myself and I do not know what is in Yourself').
This further supports the idea that the two statements are not contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورنہ یوں کہنا چاہئے تھاب خلاف ما اذا ضرب بسوط اور فلا يتحقق به الاكره صراحةً عدم تحقق اكراہ پر دال ہے پس دونوں کلام متناقض ہوئے ۔ جواب... بخلاف ما اذا اکرہ - میں اکراہ سے مراد اس کے لغوی معنی ہیں۔ یعنی حمل الانسان علی امریکر ہہ اور یہ معنی صور مذکور میں بلا شک وشبہ متحقق ہیں اور فلای تحقق بہ الاکراہ سے جس اکراہ کی نفی کی ہے وہ شرعی معنی کے لحاظ سے ہے جس پر اس کے احکام مرتب ہوتے ہیں۔ فلا تناقض یہ بھی کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ شروع میں اکراہ سے تعبیر کرنا بطریق مشاکلت ہے۔ جیسے قول باری تعالیٰ میں ہے۔ تعلم ما في نفسي ولا اعلم ما في نفسک اس صورت میں لفظ اکراہ حقیقت نہ ہو گا نہ لغویہ اور نہ شرعیہ، بلکہ مجاز ہو جائے گا۔ فلا تناقض
Otherwise, it should have been said contrary to the case where he struck with a whip? And 'so coercion is not realized by it' clearly indicates the non-realization of coercion, so both statements are contradictory. The answer: In 'contrary to the case where he coerced', what is meant by coercion is its literal meaning, i.e., compelling a person to something hated, and this meaning is undoubtedly realized in the mentioned situations. And the denial of coercion in 'so coercion is not realized by it' is from the perspective of the legal meaning, upon which its rulings are based. Thus there is no contradiction. It can also be said that the initial use of the word 'coercion' is by way of mushaakalah (congruence), like in the saying of Allah Most High: 'You know what is in myself and I do not know what is in Yourself.' In this case, the word 'coercion' would be neither literal (linguistic nor legal) but rather metaphorical. Thus there is no contradiction.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement resolving an apparent contradiction in the text of the Hidayah. The issue arises from two statements: one saying 'contrary to the case where he struck with a whip' implying that striking with a whip constitutes coercion, and another saying 'coercion is not realized by it' denying that striking with a whip is coercion. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between the literal (lughawi) and legal (shar'i) meanings of ikrah. In the literal sense, any compulsion (including a single lash or a day's imprisonment) is coercion. However, in the legal sense, such minor threats do not constitute coercion because the rulings of coercion (such as invalidating contracts) are attached only to the legal definition. Thus, the first statement uses the literal meaning, and the second uses the legal meaning. An alternative explanation is that the word 'coercion' is used metaphorically (by way of mushaakalah), similar to the Qur'anic verse where Allah uses the word 'know' in a reciprocal manner though His knowledge is different from human knowledge.

Key Terms
اکراہ (ikrāh): In this passage, ikrah is discussed in two senses: literal (lughawi) meaning any compulsion, and legal (shar'i) meaning compulsion that carries legal consequences (invalidating contracts).
مشاکلت (mushākalat): A rhetorical device where a word is used in a non-literal sense for stylistic congruence with another word in the context, akin to the Qur'anic usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the contradiction between the statement that a whip strike constitutes coercion and the statement that coercion is not realized by it?
A) By declaring one statement abrogated
B) By distinguishing literal and legal meanings of coercion
C) By rejecting one of the statements as weak
D) By saying the texts are contradictory and cannot be reconciled

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction in the Hidayah about coercion is resolved by distinguishing the literal meaning (any compulsion) from the legal meaning (compulsion with legal consequences); thus no real contradiction exists.

Exception for Dignified Persons in Coercion
States that even a minor threat is coercive for a person of high dignity like a judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a minor threat qualifies as coercion (ikrah) for a dignified person and explain the Hanafi rationale based on personal status.

Core Idea
Coercion depends on dignity: even a minor threat humiliates a high-status person, making it coercive.

In Hanafi fiqh, a threat of a single lash or one day's imprisonment is generally not considered coercion (ikrah). However, an exception exists for persons of high social standing, such as a judge or notable, for whom such a minor threat causes humiliation and disgrace. This lesson examines that exception and the principle of relativity in coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
In coercion (ikrah), a threat of minor harm (one lash or one day) is usually insufficient to cause fear of severe harm, thus not considered coercion.
This is the baseline rule from which the exception is made.
2
Recognize the exception
The text states that for a 'wajih' (dignified person), even such minor threats are humiliating and therefore constitute coercion.
The key term here is 'وجیہ' (wajih) – a person of high social standing.
3
Understand the basis
The criterion is not only objective severity but also subjective humiliation: a public reprimand can be more damaging to a respected person.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that coercive threats are evaluated relative to the person and context.
4
Note the cross-madhhab context
Some Shafi'is, Malikis, and a Hanbali narration agree with this exception, while another Hanbali narration rejects it. Qadi Shurayh's view supports relativity.
This demonstrates that the ruling is not universally accepted among all schools.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a qadi is threatened with one day in prison, that threat is ikrah in Hanafi law, affecting validity of contracts made under duress.
The practical effect: contracts signed under such coercion are fasid (defective).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله الا اذا كان الرجل .... الخ ۔ ہاں اگر وہ مگر شخص جس کو ضرب سوط یا حبس یوم کی دھمکی دی گئی ہے قاضی وغیرہ کے مانند وجیہ ومعزز ہو جس کے حق میں ایک روز کی قید یا ایک کوڑا بھی بے عزتی ہے۔ حتی کہ مجلس و مجمع میں اس کی گوشمالی بھی باعث اہانت ہے تو اس کے حق میں اتنی معمولی تہدید بھی اکراہ ہے۔ بعض شوافع ، امام مالک اور ایک روایت میں امام احمد بھی اسی کے قائل ہیں اور دوسری روایت یہ ہے کہ یہ وعیدا کراہ نہیں ہے۔ قاضی شریح سے منقول ہے کہ قید و وعید اور ضرب وشتم لوگوں کے اختلافات حالات کے اعتبار سے اکراہ ہے۔
His saying: 'Except when the man... etc.' Yes, if that person who is threatened with a whip strike or a day's imprisonment is a dignified and honored person like a judge, for whom one day's imprisonment or one lash is also a disgrace, such that even a reprimand in a gathering is humiliating for him, then in his case such a minor threat is coercion. Some Shafi'is, Imam Malik, and in one narration Imam Ahmad also hold this view, and another narration is that this threat is not coercion. It is narrated from Qadi Shurayh that imprisonment, threat, beating, and abuse are coercion depending on the differences in people and circumstances.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage expands on an exception from the general rule that a minor threat (a single lash or one day's imprisonment) is not coercion. The exception is for a person of high social standing ('wajih') such as a judge, for whom even such a minor act would cause humiliation and dishonor. The commentary notes that some Shafi'is, Imam Malik, and in one narration Imam Ahmad agree with this exception. However, another narration from Imam Ahmad states that such a threat is not coercion. Qadi Shurayh is quoted as saying that what constitutes coercion varies with people and circumstances. This highlights the Hanafi principle that coercion is relative and depends on the individual's status and the context, not solely on the objective severity of the threat.

Key Terms
وجیہ (wajīh): A person of high social standing and dignity, such as a judge or notable, for whom even minor threats are humiliating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the threat is made by a public official
B) When the threatened person is a dignified person like a judge
C) When the threat is accompanied by physical abuse
D) When the threat is made in a private setting

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a minor threat can be coercion if it humiliates a person of high social standing.

Coerced Acknowledgment is Invalid
Ruling on coerced acknowledgment with grammatical parsing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why coerced acknowledgment (iqrar) is invalid in Hanafi fiqh and explain the role of consent and likelihood of truthfulness.

Core Idea
Coerced acknowledgment is invalid because the context of duress makes it unreliable.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that acknowledgment made under coercion is legally ineffective. Unlike voluntary acknowledgment where truth is presumed, coercion introduces doubt about veracity. The passage also clarifies a grammatical point about the text's structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is acknowledgment (iqrar) made under coercion. The ruling is that it is invalid (batil) because it lacks the condition of consent.
2
Understand the reasoning
Acknowledgment can be true or false. In a state of consent, context indicates truth because a person does not normally lie against themselves. Under coercion, there is a strong possibility of falsehood to avoid harm, so the acknowledgment is unreliable.
The key is doubt (ihtimal) about truthfulness, not lack of capacity.
3
Recognize the textual structure
The commentary discusses whether the phrase 'وَكَذَا الْإِقْرَارُ' (and similarly the acknowledgment) is conjoined only to the previous clause about coercion eliminating consent, or to the entire phrase from 'لِأَنَّ مِنْ شَرْطِ الصِّحَّةِ' to 'فَيَفْسُدُ'.
This parsing does not change the ruling but clarifies the author's intended scope.
4
Apply the principle
Understand that Hanafi law requires voluntary consent for a valid acknowledgment. Any severe coercion (threat of killing, severe beating, prolonged imprisonment) invalidates the iqrar.
Mild coercion may not invalidate; the standard is severe duress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وكذا الاقرار الخ - نہایہ ، عنایہ، بنایہ میں ہے کہ یہ قول سابق و الاكراه بهذه الاشياء يعدم الرضاء فيفسد پر معطوف ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ قتل و ضرب شدید اور حبس مدید کی تہدید سے اقرار بھی فاسد ہو جائے گا۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ اقرار فی نفسہ صدق و کذب کے درمیان دائر ہے۔ لیکن رضاء و خوشدلی کی صورت میں بدلالت حال کی جانب صدق راج ہوتی ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ آدمی اپنے جی کی تکذیب نہیں کرتا اور اکراہ کی حالت میں یہ احتمال ہے کہ شاید اس نے دفع ضرر کی خاطر جھوٹا اقرار کیا ہو ۔ صاحب نتائج کی رائے یہ ہے کہ قول مذکور لان من شرط صحة سے فیفسد تک پورے مجموعہ پر معطوف ہے۔
His saying: 'And similarly the acknowledgment, etc.' - In al-Nihayah, al-Inayah, al-Binayah, it is that this clause is conjoined to the previous statement 'and coercion through these things eliminates consent, thus it is invalid.' The meaning is that acknowledgment is also invalidated when there is a threat of killing, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment. The reason is that acknowledgment in itself is between truth and falsehood. However, in a state of consent and willingness, by indication of the situation, truth prevails, because the apparent is that a person does not lie against himself. In a state of coercion, it is possible that he made a false acknowledgment for the sake of repelling harm. The author of al-Nata'ij is of the opinion that the mentioned statement is conjoined to the entire phrase from 'because from the conditions of validity' to 'thus it is invalid.'
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise that clarifies the meaning and grammatical structure of the text. It states that coerced acknowledgment (iqrar) is invalid, similar to coerced contracts. The reasoning is that acknowledgment can be true or false. When made voluntarily, the context indicates truth because one would not ordinarily lie against oneself. Under coercion, there is a strong possibility that the acknowledgment is false, made to avoid harm. The commentary also notes a disagreement about the grammatical scope of the phrase: whether it is conjoined only to the previous clause or to a longer phrase. The conclusion is that coerced acknowledgment is legally ineffective.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrār): A voluntary acknowledgment of a right or debt against oneself; under coercion it is invalid because of doubt in its truthfulness.
اكراه (ikrāh): Coercion, duress; severe threats that eliminate free consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a coerced acknowledgment considered invalid by Hanafis?
A) Because coercion eliminates the capacity to speak
B) Because there is a strong possibility of falsehood to avoid harm
C) Because acknowledgment requires a judge
D) Because it is a criminal act

Key Takeaway
Coerced acknowledgment is invalid because the context of duress makes its truth unreliable.

Coercion and the Quranic Exception: Ibahah or Raf' al-Ithm?
Refutes objection that uttering kufr under duress is permissible like carrion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i interpretations of the Quranic exception for coercion and explain why the Hanafi school holds that uttering kufr under duress remains haram.

Core Idea
The Quranic exception for coercion removes punishment, not prohibition—the act remains haram.

A common objection to the Hanafi position on coercion is that the Quranic exception 'except one who is coerced' should make uttering kufr permissible, just as the exception for carrion makes it permissible. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response: the exception only lifts the threat of punishment, not the prohibition itself. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi theory of 'azimah and rukhsah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the opponent's claim: the Quranic exception for coercion should make uttering kufr permissible (mubah) like the exception for carrion and wine.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that uttering kufr under duress remains haram; the exception only removes the punishment (qisas) for the act.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse structure
Understand the Hanafi reading: the exception is attached to the threatened punishment, not to the prohibition. The verse means: 'Whoever disbelieves… upon them is wrath… except one who is coerced…' – so the wrath is removed, not the prohibition.
This is a theological-linguistic argument about precedence and delay (taqdim wa ta'khir).
4
Contrast with the carrion exception
In the carrion verse, the exception 'except what you are compelled to necessity' directly modifies the prohibition, making the act permissible. Here, the exception modifies the consequence, not the ruling.
5
Apply the distinction to a scenario
Consider a Muslim threatened with death to renounce Islam. If he utters kufr with a calm heart, he does not incur punishment but still commits a sin. The superior course ('azimah) is to endure martyrdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و لان الحرمة .. الخ صبر کرنے پر مستحق اجر ہونے کی عقلی دلیل ہے کہ کفر کے فتیح ہونے کی وجہ سے حرمت باقی ہے تو وہ اکراہ پر صبر کر کے محرم شرعی سے مجتنب اور شرعی نہی کی تعمیل کرنے والا ہوا اور اعزاز دین کی خاطر اظہار کلمۂ کفر سے باز رہنا عزم قوی کا کام ہے۔ اور اس میں کوئی شک ہی نہیں کر رخصت اختیار کرنے کی بہ نسبت عزیمت پر عمل کرنا افضل ہے بخلاف اکل میتہ وشرب خمر کے کہ اس میں بوجہ استثناء ( الا ما اضطررتم ) اباحت ہو گئی۔ لان الاستثناء من التحريم اباحة ..... اس دلیل پر یہ اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ جیسے مسئلہ میتہ میں مذکورہ استثناء کے ذریعہ سے حالت ضرورت مستثنی ایسے ہی یہاں الا من اکره و قلبه مطمئن بالایمان استثناء کے ذریعہ سے حالت اکراہ بھی مستفی ہے۔ پس اکل مدیتہ و شرب خمر کی طرح اجراء کلمہ کفر بھی مباح ہونا چاہئے۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ آیت میں تقدیم و تاخیر ہے، تقدیر کلام یوں ہے من كفر بالله من بعد ايمانه و شرح بالكفر ضدرًا فعليهم غضب من الله ولهم عذاب عظيم الا من اكره و قلبه مطئمن بالایمان پس اکراہ کی حالت میں حق تعالیٰ نے اجراء کلمہ کفر کو مباح نہیں کیا بلکہ اس حالت میں ان سے عذاب وغضب کو اٹھا دیا اور غضب کی نفی سے جو حرمت کا حکم ہے عدم حرمت لازم نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ عدم حکم کی ضروریات میں سے عدم علت نہیں ہے جیسے مسافر و مریض کے حق میں شہو رمضان کا سبب موجود ہے اور حکم متاخر ہے۔
His statement: 'and because of the prohibition...' etc. is a rational proof for being deserving of reward upon patience: because due to the opening of disbelief, the prohibition remains, so he, by patient endurance of coercion, refrains from the legally prohibited and complies with the legal command, and refraining from uttering the word of disbelief for the honor of the religion is a matter of strong resolve. There is no doubt that acting upon 'azimah (strict adherence) is superior to adopting rukhsah (concession), unlike eating carrion and drinking wine, where due to the exception (except what you are compelled to necessity) permission (ibahah) occurs. Because the exception from prohibition is permission. This proof is objected to: just as in the case of carrion, the mentioned exception exempts the state of necessity, similarly here, the exception 'except one who is coerced while his heart is at peace with faith' also exempts the state of coercion. So uttering the word of disbelief should be permissible like eating carrion and drinking wine. The answer is that in the verse there is precedence and delay; the intended meaning is: 'Whoever disbelieves in Allah after his belief and opens his chest to disbelief, upon them is wrath from Allah and for them is a great punishment, except one who is coerced while his heart is at peace with faith.' Thus, in the state of coercion, Allah did not make the utterance of kufr permissible, but rather He removed punishment and wrath from them in that state. And from the negation of wrath, which is a ruling of prohibition, the negation of prohibition does not necessarily follow, because the negation of a ruling is not among the necessities of the negation of the cause, just as in the case of the traveler and the sick, the cause for Ramadan fasting exists but the ruling is delayed.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing a potential objection to the Hanafi position on coercion in matters of faith. The commentator notes that the Quranic exception (except one who is coerced) does not create permission (ibahah) to utter kufr, unlike the exception for carrion and wine (except when compelled by necessity). Instead, it merely removes the punishment (qisas) for the act, leaving the prohibition intact. This is because the wording of the verse ('except one who is coerced') is syntactically connected to the threatened punishment, not to the prohibition. The commentator draws an analogy with the traveler and sick person: the cause for fasting exists (Ramadan) but the ruling (obligation to fast) is delayed (they may fast later). Thus, the act of uttering kufr under duress remains prohibited, though the one who does so with a firm heart is not punished. However, the preferred course ('azimah) is to endure patience and refrain, even at the risk of death, as this demonstrates strong resolve for the religion.

Key Terms
اكره (ukriha): Coerced under threat of death or severe harm; the state of duress that excuses from punishment but does not make the act permissible.
استثناء (istithna'): An exception from a general ruling; here the exception in the Quranic verse is argued to apply to the punishment, not to the prohibition itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, what is the legal effect of the Quranic exception (except one who is coerced) for uttering kufr?
A) It makes uttering kufr permissible (mubah)
B) It removes the prohibition (haram)
C) It removes the punishment but the act remains prohibited
D) It categorically forbids uttering kufr under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between an exception that creates permissibility (as in carrion) and an exception that only lifts punishment (as in coercion); uttering kufr under duress remains haram.

Coercion to Destroy Property and to Kill: Rulings and Liability
Ruling on destroying property and killing under duress with liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying property and killing under duress, and identify who bears liability in each case.

Core Idea
Necessity permits destroying property but never taking a life; the coercer bears liability for both.

This lesson from Al-Hidayah examines two scenarios of coercion: when the coerced is forced to destroy another's property or to kill another person. The key distinction is between harm to property and harm to life. While property can be sacrificed under threat to life or limb, taking an innocent life remains absolutely prohibited even under threat of death. The Hanafi school further clarifies that the coercer is legally responsible for the consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage covers two distinct cases: coercion to destroy property and coercion to kill. Note the different rulings for each.
The ruling on property is mubah; on killing it is haram.
2
Analyze the qiyas for property destruction
The permissibility is analogized (qiyas) to the state of famine (makhmasah) where consuming another's property is allowed due to necessity. Identify the illa: necessity (darurah) that jeopardizes life or limb.
This is a clear application of the principle that necessity permits what is normally prohibited (al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat).
3
Understand the distinction between property and life
Killing a Muslim is never permissible under any necessity, unlike property. Note the strong prohibition: the coerced must be patient even unto death.
This shows that the sanctity of life overrides even extreme duress.
4
Examine the ikhtilaf on qisas
When a coerced killing occurs, there is ikhtilaf on who bears qisas. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds the coercer liable; Zufar says the coerced; Abu Yusuf says neither; al-Shafi'i says both.
The mufta bihi opinion is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Consider how these rules apply today: a person forced at gunpoint to destroy property or to kill. The ruling remains the same; the coerced instrument is not liable for property damage, but the coercer is; for killing, the coerced is sinful but qisas is on the coercer.
This highlights the distinction between direct and indirect causation in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان اكره علی اتلاف مال مسلم بامر يخاف منه على نفسه او علي عضو من اعضائه وسعه ان يفعل ذلك لان مال الغير يستباح لضرورة كما فى حالة المخمصة وقد تحققت ولصاحب المال ان يضمن المكره لان المكرة آلة للمكره فيما يصلح آلة له والاتلاف من هذا القبيل وان اكره بقتل على قتل غيره لم يسعه ان يُقدم عليه ويصبر حتى يقتل فان قتله كان آثما لان قتل المسلم مما لا يستباح لضرورة ما فكذا بهذه الضرورة والقصاص على المكره ان كان القتل عمدا قال وهذا عند ابي حنيفة ومحمد وقال زفر على المكره وقال ابويوسف لا يجب عليهما وقال الشافعى يجب عليهما
He said: If one is coerced to destroy the property of a Muslim by something he fears for his life or a limb, it is permissible for him to do that, because the property of another is permissible due to necessity just as in the state of famine (makhmasah), and it has been realized. And the owner of the property may hold the coercer (mukrih) liable, because the coerced person is an instrument for the coercer in what is suitable as an instrument, and destruction is of this category. And if he is coerced by death (threat) to kill another, it is not permissible for him to proceed; he should be patient until he is killed. If he kills him, he is sinful, because killing a Muslim is not permissible for any necessity, so similarly for this necessity. And the retaliation is upon the coercer if the killing was intentional. He said: This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Zufar said: upon the coerced. Abu Yusuf said: not upon both. Al-Shafi'i said: upon both.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on two scenarios of coercion. First, if a person is threatened with death or loss of a limb unless he destroys another Muslim's property, it is permissible to destroy the property. This is based on necessity, analogized to the state of pressing hunger (makhmasah) where consuming another's property is allowed. The coercer (mukrih) is liable for compensation because the coerced person acts as a mere instrument. Second, if threatened with death to kill another person, it is not permissible to kill; the person must endure patience even if it leads to his own death. If he kills, he is sinful, and retaliation (qisas) falls on the coercer (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). The passage notes the ikhtilaf: Zufar holds that qisas is on the coerced, Abu Yusuf on neither, and al-Shafi'i on both. The Hanafi position is that the murder is attributed to the coercer due to causation, while the coerced is an instrument.

Key Terms
مكرِه (mukrih): The person who compels another through threat; the coercer.
مكرَه (mukrah): The person who is coerced; the one compelled to act under threat.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation for murder where the killer is executed if the killing was intentional and unlawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, upon whom does qisas fall when a person is coerced to kill another under threat of death?
A) The coerced person (mukrah)
B) The coercer (mukrih)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, necessity permits destroying property but never taking a life, and the coercer bears legal liability.
